\midrule
AMC 10/12 & 25 each & High school & Multiple choice (5) & $\sim$95\% & Mid-tier math \\
Minerva Math & 272 & University & Open-ended & $\sim$90\% & STEM reasoning \\
APPS & 10,000 & Mixed & Pass/Fail (tests) & $\sim$80\% & Algorithmic code \\
CodeContests & 13,328 & Competition & Pass/Fail (tests) & $\sim$45\% & Competitive programming \\
MathVista & 6,141 & Mixed & Mixed & $\sim$70\% & Visual math \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Code Generation Benchmarks}

\subsubsection{HumanEval and MBPP}

HumanEval \cite{chen2021humaneval} is a benchmark of 164 hand-crafted Python programming problems introduced by OpenAI. Each problem includes a function signature, a docstring specification, and a suite of unit tests. The model must synthesize a correct function body from the docstring; evaluation uses the \emph{pass@k} metric, measuring whether at least one of $k$ generated samples passes all test cases. At release, Codex-12B achieved 28.8\% pass@1; as of early 2026, frontier models exceed 99\%.

The Mostly Basic Python Problems (MBPP) benchmark \cite{austin2021mbpp} complements HumanEval with 974 crowd-sourced Python programming tasks targeting introductory-level skills. Each task provides a natural language description and three test cases. MBPP tests a broader distribution of basic programming patterns than HumanEval. Both benchmarks have approached saturation for frontier models, leading to the development of enhanced variants (HumanEval+ and MBPP+) with expanded test suites that expose edge-case failures \cite{liu2024humanevalplus}.

\subsubsection{LiveCodeBench}

LiveCodeBench \cite{jain2024livecodebench}, published at ICLR 2025, addresses the critical problem of \emph{data contamination} in code benchmarks. It continuously collects new competitive programming problems from LeetCode, AtCoder, and Codeforces after a given cutoff date, ensuring that problems were not present in any model's training data. Beyond code generation, LiveCodeBench evaluates self-repair, code execution prediction, and test output prediction capabilities. The benchmark currently hosts over 600 problems and has been instrumental in detecting contamination across models including GPT-4o, Claude, and DeepSeek. DeepCoder-14B, a GRPO+-trained model, achieved 60.6\% on LiveCodeBench \cite{deepcoder2025}, demonstrating the applicability of RL-based training to code reasoning.

\subsubsection{SWE-bench}

SWE-bench \cite{jimenez2024swebench}, published at ICLR 2024, evaluates whether language models can resolve real-world GitHub issues by generating code patches. The benchmark sources task instances from popular open-source Python repositories, each consisting of a GitHub issue description and a test suite that validates the fix. The full benchmark contains 2,294 instances; the \emph{SWE-bench Verified} subset of 500 instances has been validated by human software engineers from OpenAI to ensure solvability and unambiguous specifications.

SWE-bench represents a qualitative shift from function-level code generation to repository-level software engineering---models must navigate multi-file codebases, understand project conventions, and produce patches that pass both new and regression tests. As of early 2026, the strongest agentic systems achieve approximately 72\% on SWE-bench Verified. SWE-bench has become the de facto standard for evaluating AI coding agents, and its multimodal and multilingual extensions further broaden the evaluation scope.

\subsection{Scientific and General Reasoning Benchmarks}

\subsubsection{GPQA Diamond}

The Graduate-Level Google-Proof Q\&A (GPQA) benchmark \cite{rein2024gpqa}, developed by researchers at NYU and Anthropic, evaluates expert-level scientific reasoning. The \emph{Diamond} subset consists of 198 multiple-choice questions in biology, physics, and chemistry, crafted by PhD-level domain experts and validated through a rigorous multi-stage protocol. Questions are designed to be ``Google-proof''---skilled non-experts with unrestricted internet access achieve only $\sim$34\% accuracy, while domain experts score $\sim$65--70\%.

GPQA Diamond has served as a critical benchmark for tracking the frontier of scientific reasoning in LLMs. At launch, GPT-4 achieved only $\sim$39\%. By mid-2024, OpenAI's o1 reached $\sim$77\%, surpassing human expert performance. As of early 2026, top models exceed 92\%. The benchmark's relevance to RL-trained reasoning models is significant: DeepSeek-R1 achieved 71.5\% on GPQA Diamond, demonstrating that GRPO-based training can enhance scientific reasoning beyond mathematics.

\subsubsection{MMLU and MMLU-Pro}

The Massive Multitask Language Understanding (MMLU) benchmark \cite{hendrycks2021mmlu} evaluates knowledge and reasoning across 57 diverse subjects spanning STEM, humanities, social sciences, and professional domains. Each subject contains multiple-choice questions at difficulty levels ranging from elementary to advanced professional. MMLU has been one of the most widely cited LLM benchmarks, though frontier models now routinely exceed 90\%, reducing its discriminative power.

MMLU-Pro \cite{wang2024mmlupro} addresses these limitations by expanding answer choices from 4 to 10, eliminating trivially solvable questions, and focusing on reasoning-intensive tasks. The resulting benchmark of over 12,000 questions causes a 16--33\% accuracy drop compared to original MMLU, restoring discriminative power. DeepSeek-R1 achieved 85.5\% on MMLU, demonstrating that RL-trained reasoning capabilities transfer beyond mathematics to general knowledge domains.

\subsubsection{OlympiadBench}

OlympiadBench \cite{he2024olympiadbench}, presented at ACL 2024, is a bilingual (English--Chinese) multimodal benchmark comprising 8,476 Olympiad-level problems in mathematics and physics. Unlike MATH or AIME, which are text-only, OlympiadBench includes problems with diagrams, graphs, and figures that require visual understanding. Problems are drawn from international science Olympiads and national competitions, with detailed step-by-step solutions provided for evaluation.

The benchmark is particularly relevant to the study of RL-trained reasoning models because it tests whether mathematical reasoning capabilities transfer to problems requiring both symbolic manipulation and visual interpretation. Frontier reasoning models achieve approximately 60\% accuracy, indicating substantial room for improvement and preserving discriminative power for evaluating future RL-enhanced systems.

\subsubsection{ARC-AGI}

The Abstraction and Reasoning Corpus (ARC-AGI) \cite{chollet2019arc}, introduced by Fran\c{c}ois Chollet, evaluates \emph{fluid intelligence}---the ability to solve novel problems without prior domain-specific knowledge. Each task presents a small number of input--output grid pairs as demonstrations; the model must infer the underlying transformation rule and apply it to a held-out test grid. ARC-AGI is designed to resist memorisation: tasks require recognising abstract patterns (symmetry, counting, object manipulation) that are trivial for humans but remain extremely challenging for LLMs.

ARC-AGI-1 was the benchmark that pinpointed the moment in late 2024 when AI systems moved beyond pure memorisation, with OpenAI's o3-preview achieving a breakthrough score. ARC-AGI-2, released in March 2025, is substantially harder: pure LLMs score 0\%, and public reasoning systems achieve only single-digit accuracy, while every task has been solved by at least two humans in under two attempts. ARC-AGI provides a complementary evaluation axis to mathematical benchmarks by testing general abstract reasoning rather than domain-specific knowledge.

\subsubsection{MMMU}

The Massive Multi-discipline Multimodal Understanding (MMMU) benchmark \cite{yue2024mmmu} evaluates multimodal models on 11,500 expert-level questions spanning 30 subjects across six disciplines (Art \& Design, Business, Science, Health \& Medicine, Humanities, and Technology \& Engineering). Each question requires understanding images---charts, diagrams, maps, chemical structures, medical scans, or artwork---alongside textual reasoning.

MMMU is particularly important for evaluating whether RL-trained reasoning capabilities extend to multimodal settings. At release, frontier models achieved approximately 56\%; by early 2026, the best systems reach $\sim$78\%, indicating that multimodal expert-level reasoning remains an active challenge. The benchmark has become the standard evaluation for vision-language models and is increasingly relevant as GRPO-style training is extended to multimodal architectures.

\subsection{Additional Evaluation Datasets}

\subsubsection{AMC 10/12}

The American Mathematics Competitions (AMC 10 and AMC 12) are widely-administered pre-AIME competitions consisting of 25 multiple-choice problems each, with 75-minute time limits. AMC problems are generally easier than AIME but harder than GSM8K, filling a useful difficulty gap. Problems test algebra, geometry, combinatorics, and number theory at the high school level. SimpleRL-Zoo \cite{zeng2025simplerl} uses AMC as one of its standard evaluation benchmarks, reporting that GRPO-trained models show consistent improvements across the AMC difficulty range.

\subsubsection{Minerva Math}

The Minerva Math benchmark, curated from the evaluation suite of Google's Minerva model \cite{lewkowycz2022minerva}, consists of quantitative reasoning problems drawn from STEM courses including calculus, linear algebra, probability, and physics. Problems require multi-step symbolic computation and domain knowledge beyond the competition-math style of MATH and AIME. Minerva Math has been adopted as a standard evaluation benchmark by several RL-for-reasoning works, including SimpleRL-Zoo and AceReason-Nemotron, providing coverage of university-level mathematical reasoning that complements the competition-oriented benchmarks.

\subsubsection{APPS and CodeContests}

APPS \cite{hendrycks2021apps} (Automated Programming Progress Standard) consists of 10,000 coding problems collected from open-access coding websites such as Codeforces and Kattis, spanning three difficulty levels: introductory, interview, and competition. Each problem includes multiple test cases for evaluation, and solutions require understanding of algorithms, data structures, and mathematical reasoning.

CodeContests \cite{li2022codecontests}, introduced by DeepMind, is a competitive programming benchmark drawn from Codeforces contests with additional problems from other platforms. It includes 13,328 problems with extensive test cases (both public and hidden). Both benchmarks evaluate a model's ability to translate natural language problem descriptions into correct programs, testing algorithmic reasoning---a capability closely related to the chain-of-thought reasoning that GRPO training aims to enhance.

\subsubsection{MathVista}

MathVista \cite{lu2024mathvista} is a consolidated mathematical reasoning benchmark designed to evaluate models on tasks requiring \emph{visual} mathematical understanding. It aggregates 6,141 problems from 28 existing datasets and 3 newly created datasets, spanning five primary mathematical reasoning types: algebraic reasoning, arithmetic reasoning, geometry reasoning, numeric common sense, and statistical reasoning. Problems include function plots, geometric diagrams, tables, bar charts, and scientific figures.

MathVista bridges the gap between text-only math benchmarks (GSM8K, MATH) and general multimodal benchmarks (MMMU), providing targeted evaluation of whether mathematical reasoning capabilities extend to visual inputs. At release, GPT-4V achieved 49.9\%; as of early 2026, frontier multimodal reasoning models reach approximately 70\%, indicating ongoing challenges in visual mathematical reasoning.


%% ================================================================
%% Section 2.9: Related Work on RL for LLM Reasoning
%% ================================================================
\section{Related Work on RL for LLM Reasoning}
\label{sec:related-work-rl-reasoning}

The release of DeepSeek-R1 in January 2025 ignited a wave of research into RL as a mechanism for eliciting and scaling reasoning capabilities. This section surveys the most influential concurrent efforts.

\subsection{DeepScaleR: Iterative Context Lengthening}

DeepScaleR~\cite{luo2025deepscaler}, developed at UC Berkeley and Agentica, demonstrated that RL can be scaled effectively even with a \emph{1.5B parameter} model. The central innovation is \textbf{iterative context lengthening}: training begins with shorter context windows (8K tokens) and progressively extends to 16K and 24K. DeepScaleR-1.5B achieved \textbf{43.1\% Pass@1 on AIME 2024}, matching OpenAI's o1-preview. The training dataset comprised $\sim$40,000 math problems, and the entire recipe was open-sourced.

\subsection{SimpleRL-Zoo: Systematic Investigation of Zero RL Training}

SimpleRL-Zoo~\cite{zeng2025simplerl}, published at COLM 2025, studied zero RL training across \textbf{10 diverse base models} spanning different families and scales. Key findings: (i)~most prior reproduction efforts focused on Qwen2.5 models, which are unrepresentatively favorable starting points; (ii)~the ``aha moment'' was observed for the first time in small models outside the Qwen family; (iii)~base model quality is often more important than scale alone.

\subsection{TinyZero: Minimal Reproduction of R1-Zero Reasoning}

TinyZero~\cite{wen2025tinyzero} reproduced the R1-Zero paradigm at the \textbf{3B parameter} scale on the Countdown number game for under \$30 of compute. The 3B base model spontaneously developed self-verification, systematic search, and iterative revision---demonstrating the minimal conditions under which RL can induce genuine reasoning.

\subsection{DeepCoder: Extending RL Reasoning to Code Generation}

DeepCoder~\cite{xu2025deepcoder} extended the RL scaling paradigm to competitive programming, achieving \textbf{60.6\% Pass@1 on LiveCodeBench} with a 14B model using GRPO+ (enhanced GRPO with DAPO-inspired modifications). The approach adopted iterative context lengthening and sparse binary rewards from test case execution.

\subsection{Open-Reasoner-Zero and Other Reproductions}

Open-Reasoner-Zero demonstrated that vanilla PPO with rule-based rewards---without KL regularization---achieved superior performance to DeepSeek-R1-Zero-Qwen-32B while requiring only 1/10 of the training steps. Other efforts include Dr.\ GRPO (correcting GRPO's length bias), Sky-T1 (achieving o1-preview performance with minimal resources), and Logic-RL (extending RL training to logical reasoning).

\subsection{Scaling Trends in RL Post-Training}

Tan et al.\ conducted a comprehensive investigation across the Qwen2.5 dense model series (0.5B--72B), establishing that: (1)~under fixed compute, larger models trained for fewer RL steps outperform smaller models trained longer; (2)~RL post-training follows predictable power-law scaling; (3)~data reuse is effective, as final RL performance is governed by total optimization steps rather than sample uniqueness.


%% ================================================================
%% Section 2.10: Infrastructure for RL Training of LLMs
%% ================================================================
\section{Infrastructure for RL Training of LLMs}
\label{sec:infrastructure-rl-training}

RL training introduces infrastructure demands beyond supervised fine-tuning: on-policy sampling, reward scoring, and gradient computation must share or synchronize model weights. This section surveys the key tools.

\subsection{TRL: Transformer Reinforcement Learning}

TRL \cite{vonwerra2022trl} provides a unified interface for post-training with PPO, DPO, GRPO, and more. Its \texttt{GRPOTrainer} class manages the full RL loop and supports custom reward functions, with distributed training via DeepSpeed and FSDP.

\subsection{OpenRLHF}

OpenRLHF is a high-performance distributed RLHF framework combining Ray, vLLM, and DeepSpeed. It supports PPO, REINFORCE++, GRPO, RLOO, and DAPO algorithms, with tight vLLM integration for high-throughput on-policy generation.

\subsection{DeepSpeed and FSDP}

DeepSpeed's ZeRO optimizer \cite{rajbhandari2020zero} partitions optimizer states (Stage 1), gradients (Stage 2), and parameters (Stage 3) across data-parallel ranks, reducing per-GPU memory consumption to $O(1/N)$. PyTorch FSDP provides a native alternative. Both are critical enablers for RL training where multiple large models must coexist in memory.

\subsection{vLLM}

vLLM \cite{kwon2023vllm} addresses the inference bottleneck through PagedAttention and continuous batching, achieving 10--24$\times$ higher throughput than naive inference. In RL training, generation can account for up to 90\% of wall-clock time, making vLLM essential for practical GRPO training.

\subsection{Tinker: Training-as-a-Service}

Tinker, developed by Thinking Machines Lab, exposes a cloud-based Python API with four primitive operations: \texttt{forward\_backward}, \texttt{optim\_step}, \texttt{sample}, and \texttt{save\_state}. Researchers write training loops locally on CPU while Tinker orchestrates distributed GPU computation. The platform supports models from Qwen3.5-4B to Qwen3.5-397B-A17B via LoRA fine-tuning, significantly democratizing access to RL research.

\subsection{Atropos and the Tinker--Atropos Integration}

Atropos, developed by Nous Research, is an environment microservice framework that decouples environments, trajectory batching, and training into independently deployable components. The \emph{tinker-atropos} integration connects Atropos environments to Tinker's managed compute, enabling any Atropos environment to leverage Tinker's GPU infrastructure. Experimental results on GSM8k demonstrate comparable reward convergence with lower per-step training time due to asynchronous decoupling of inference and gradient computation.


%% ================================================================
%% Section 2.11: Scaling Laws and Empirical Observations
%% ================================================================
\section{Scaling Laws and Empirical Observations}
\label{sec:scaling-laws}

\subsection{Classical Scaling Laws for Pretraining}

Kaplan et al.\ established that test loss obeys smooth power-law relationships \cite{kaplan2020scaling}:
\begin{equation}
  L(N) = \left(\frac{N_c}{N}\right)^{\!\alpha_N}\!,\qquad
  L(D) = \left(\frac{D_c}{D}\right)^{\!\alpha_D}\!,\qquad
  L(C) = \left(\frac{C_c}{C}\right)^{\!\alpha_C}\!,
  \label{eq:kaplan-power-laws}
\end{equation}
where $N$ denotes parameters, $D$ training tokens, $C$ compute, with exponents $\alpha_N \approx 0.076$, $\alpha_D \approx 0.095$, $\alpha_C \approx 0.050$.

\subsection{Compute-Optimal Training: Chinchilla Scaling}

Hoffmann et al.\ showed that model size and training tokens should be scaled equally \cite{hoffmann2022chinchilla}:
\begin{equation}
  L(N, D) = \frac{A}{N^{\alpha}} + \frac{B}{D^{\beta}} + E,
  \label{eq:chinchilla}
\end{equation}
with $\alpha \approx 0.34$, $\beta \approx 0.28$. Under fixed compute $C \propto 6ND$, optimal allocation scales both $N$ and $D$ proportionally. Chinchilla (70B, 4$\times$ more data) uniformly outperformed Gopher (280B).

\subsection{Scaling Laws for Reinforcement Learning Fine-Tuning}

RL training exhibits \emph{sigmoidal} rather than power-law performance curves. Khatri et al.\ proposed:
\begin{equation}
  R_C - R_0 = (A - R_0) \cdot \frac{1}{1 + (C_{\text{mid}} / C)^B},
  \label{eq:rl-sigmoid}
\end{equation}
where $A$ is the asymptotic pass rate and $B$ governs compute efficiency. Key findings: (1)~not all RL recipes reach the same ceiling; (2)~methods superior at small compute can be inferior at scale; (3)~scaling model size substantially improves both asymptotic performance and downstream generalization.

The effect of \emph{group size} $G$ is a scaling dimension unique to GRPO. DeepSeek-R1 used $G = 64$; academic reproductions find $G \in [8, 32]$ offers a practical trade-off.

\subsection{Test-Time Compute Scaling}

Snell et al.\ showed that compute-optimal test-time scaling---adaptively allocating inference compute per prompt---can achieve $>$4$\times$ improvement over uniform best-of-$N$ baselines \cite{snell2024scaling}. A smaller model with optimal test-time scaling outperformed a 14$\times$ larger model in zero-shot mode. This connects directly to R1-style long chain-of-thought reasoning, where RL teaches models to allocate their own test-time compute.

\subsection{Reward Hacking and Overoptimisation}

Gao et al.\ characterised reward model overoptimisation \cite{gao2023scaling}: gold reward initially increases with KL divergence from the reference policy but eventually peaks and declines. Larger proxy reward models delay overoptimisation onset but do not eliminate it. For GRPO with rule-based rewards, overoptimisation can still manifest through formatting tricks or overfitting to training prompt distributions.

\subsection{Implications for the Present Study}

This capstone focuses on the effect of base model size on GRPO training dynamics, holding the algorithm (GRPO), adaptation method (LoRA), training framework (TRL \cite{vonwerra2022trl}), reward structure (rule-based verification), and benchmarks (GSM8K, MATH) constant. This design isolates what the scaling literature identifies as one of the most consequential variables, aiming to empirically locate the transition point below which distillation dominates direct RL, and to provide actionable guidance for practitioners under resource constraints.


%% ================================================================
%% Section 2.12: Summary
%% ================================================================
\section{Summary}
\label{sec:lit-summary}

This chapter has traced the intellectual lineage of reinforcement learning for large language models, from its earliest formulations through to the latest open-source reproductions.

The foundational paradigm of \textbf{RLHF with PPO} established that human preference signals could steer pretrained language models toward helpful behaviour~\cite{ouyang2022instructgpt}, but carried substantial engineering complexity: a four-model orchestration with extensive hyperparameter tuning~\cite{schulman2017ppo, zheng2023secrets}.

\textbf{Direct Preference Optimization (DPO)} offered an elegant shortcut by collapsing the RLHF pipeline into supervised learning on preference pairs~\cite{rafailov2023dpo}, yet introduced dependence on static data and the absence of online exploration.

\textbf{Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO)}~\cite{shao2024deepseekmath} charted a middle path, replacing learned value estimation with group-relative advantage normalization. The resulting algorithm retained PPO's clipped surrogate objective and KL regularisation while halving the number of trainable models.

\textbf{DeepSeek-R1}~\cite{deepseek2025r1} demonstrated that pure GRPO training with rule-based rewards could elicit emergent reasoning---self-verification, reflection, and extended chains of thought---without curated human demonstrations.

\textbf{Parameter-efficient fine-tuning} via LoRA~\cite{hu2021lora} and QLoRA~\cite{dettmers2023qlora} has been indispensable in democratising access to RL training, making GRPO feasible on academic-grade hardware.

\textbf{Evaluation benchmarks} spanning mathematical reasoning (GSM8K, MATH, AIME, AMC, Minerva Math, OlympiadBench, MathVista), code generation (HumanEval, LiveCodeBench, SWE-bench, APPS, CodeContests), and scientific/general reasoning (GPQA Diamond, MMLU-Pro, MMMU, ARC-AGI) collectively provide verifiable ground-truth answers enabling rule-based reward computation and comprehensive capability assessment.

A \textbf{growing ecosystem of open-source reproductions}---TinyZero~\cite{wen2025tinyzero}, SimpleRL-Zoo~\cite{zeng2025simplerl}, DeepScaleR~\cite{luo2025deepscaler}, and DeepCoder~\cite{xu2025deepcoder}---has validated that GRPO-based training can be replicated at various scales using open data and commodity hardware.

\paragraph{Positioning of this study.} The present capstone project contributes to this landscape by conducting a controlled, systematic investigation of how GRPO training dynamics, emergent reasoning behaviours, and downstream mathematical performance scale across model sizes. By holding the algorithm (GRPO), the adaptation method (LoRA), the training framework (TRL~\cite{vonwerra2022trl}), and the evaluation benchmarks constant while varying model capacity, this study isolates the effect of scale on reinforcement-learned reasoning---a dimension that existing work has explored only incidentally.


%% ================================================================
%% BIBLIOGRAPHY
%% ================================================================
\bibliography{references}

\part{Survey Chapters}

\chapter{Foundations and Alignment Algorithms}
\label{ch:foundations}

\textit{This chapter covers the three dominant algorithm families for training agentic AI: PPO-style RLHF (\S\ref{sec:taxonomy}), DPO-style direct optimization (\S\ref{sec:dpo}), and GRPO-style group comparison (\S\ref{sec:grpo}). We present a quantitative analysis of algorithm adoption and provide selection guidelines.}

\vspace{0.3cm}

\noindent\textit{Mathematical notation used in this chapter follows Table~\ref{tab:notation}.}

\section{Mechanisms of RLHF}
The primary challenge is that gradient descent needs something to optimize, but ``make this response more helpful'' isn't a loss function you can differentiate. You can't write down an equation for what makes one answer better than another---ask ten people and you'll get ten different rankings. \citet{christiano2017deep} figured out a workaround: don't ask humans to score responses on some scale (they're terrible at it), just ask them to pick which response is better. Collect enough of these A-vs-B comparisons and you can train a neural network to approximate the pattern in those choices. It's a noisy proxy for what people actually want, but it's enough to give gradient descent something concrete to chase.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    stage/.style={rectangle, draw, fill=green!20, text width=2.2cm, text centered, minimum height=1.4cm, rounded corners},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth, very thick}
]
    \node[stage] (base) {Base Model\\{\scriptsize(Llama, Qwen)}};
    \node[stage, right=2cm of base] (sft) {SFT\\{\scriptsize(Supervised\\Fine-Tuning)}};
    \node[stage, right=2cm of sft] (rl) {RL Tuning\\{\scriptsize(PPO/DPO/\\GRPO)}};
    \node[stage, right=2cm of rl] (agent) {Fine-tuned\\Agent};

    \draw[arrow] (base) -- node[above, font=\scriptsize] {Demos} (sft);
    \draw[arrow] (sft) -- node[above, font=\scriptsize] {Rewards} (rl);
    \draw[arrow] (rl) -- node[above, font=\scriptsize] {Deploy} (agent);

    % Annotations
    \node[below=0.3cm of sft, font=\tiny] {5K--10K demos};
    \node[below=0.3cm of rl, font=\tiny] {Environment interaction};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Three-stage training for agentic systems. \textbf{Stage 1:} A base model supplies language understanding. \textbf{Stage 2:} Imitation learning on 5K--10K examples drills task formats. \textbf{Stage 3:} RL tunes the policy through environment interaction, letting agents cope with novel situations outside their training distribution.}
\label{fig:rl_pipeline}
\source{Based on DeepSeek-R1 methodology~\cite{deepseek2025r1}}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[scale=0.9, transform shape]
    % === AGENT BOX ===
    \node[draw, thick, fill=blue!20, rounded corners, minimum width=4cm, minimum height=2.5cm] (agent) at (0, 2.5) {};
    \node[font=\bfseries] at (0, 3.3) {Agent};

    % Agent internals
    \node[draw, fill=blue!10, rounded corners, font=\scriptsize, minimum width=1.5cm] (policy) at (-0.8, 2.3) {Policy $\pi_\theta$};
    \node[draw, fill=blue!10, rounded corners, font=\scriptsize, minimum width=1.5cm] (value) at (0.8, 2.3) {Value $V_\phi$};

    % === ENVIRONMENT BOX ===
    \node[draw, thick, fill=green!20, rounded corners, minimum width=4cm, minimum height=2cm] (env) at (0, -1.5) {};
    \node[font=\bfseries] at (0, -0.8) {Environment};
    \node[font=\scriptsize, gray] at (0, -1.8) {(Web, Code, OS, APIs)};

    % === ARROWS WITH LABELS ===
    % Action arrow (Agent → Environment)
    \draw[->, thick, red!70!black, line width=1.5pt] (1.5, 1.1) -- (1.5, -0.3);
    \node[right, font=\small, red!70!black] at (1.6, 0.4) {Action $a_t$};

    % State arrow (Environment → Agent)
    \draw[->, thick, blue!70!black, line width=1.5pt] (-1.5, -0.3) -- (-1.5, 1.1);
    \node[left, font=\small, blue!70!black] at (-1.6, 0.4) {State $s_{t+1}$};

    % Reward arrow (curved, from environment to agent)
    \draw[->, thick, orange!80!black, line width=1.5pt] (2.3, -1.5) to[out=0, in=0] (2.3, 2.5);
    \node[right, font=\small, orange!80!black] at (2.4, 0.5) {Reward $r_t$};

    % === TIME STEP INDICATOR ===
    \node[draw, dashed, gray, rounded corners, font=\scriptsize, inner sep=4pt] at (0, -3.2) {
        $t \rightarrow t+1$: Agent observes $s_t$, takes $a_t$, receives $r_t$, transitions to $s_{t+1}$
    };

    % === RL OBJECTIVE ===
    \node[draw, thick, fill=yellow!20, rounded corners, font=\small, align=center, inner sep=6pt] at (6, 0.5) {
        \textbf{Objective:}\\[2pt]
        $\max_\theta \mathbb{E}\left[\sum_{t=0}^{H} \gamma^t r_t\right]$
    };

\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{The reinforcement learning loop. At each timestep, the \textbf{agent} observes state $s_t$, selects action $a_t$ according to policy $\pi_\theta$, and receives reward $r_t$ from the \textbf{environment}. We want a policy that racks up as much long-run reward as possible. In the agentic setting, the ``environment'' spans web browsers, code interpreters, operating systems, and API back-ends.}
\label{fig:rl_loop}
\source{Standard RL formulation~\cite{sutton2018reinforcement}}
\end{figure}

The recipe that emerged from summarization work \cite{stiennon2020learning} goes like this:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Teach the basics:} Fine-tune your base model on demonstration data. This gets it speaking the right language and following the task format---think of it as making sure the student knows how to hold a pencil before you grade their essays.
    \item \textbf{Build a scorer:} Train a separate network $r_\phi(x, y)$ that predicts which response humans would prefer. Feed it thousands of A-vs-B comparisons; it learns to spot the patterns in what annotators picked.
    \item \textbf{Optimize against the scorer:} Run PPO to push the model toward higher scores while keeping it from wandering too far from its initialization. That KL penalty matters---without it, models find bizarre loopholes that score well but produce garbage.
\end{enumerate}

\section{Limitations of RLHF and Mitigation Strategies}

The vanilla RLHF setup has some ugly failure modes that won't go away with better hyperparameters:

\textit{Humans are slow.} Every preference judgment needs a person's eyeballs, which means your annotation budget puts a hard ceiling on training scale. Want 10$\times$ more data? Hire 10$\times$ more annotators.

\textit{Reward models can be gamed.} Push the optimization hard enough and your model will discover responses that score high but read like nonsense---the reward network learned to recognize surface patterns, not actual quality.

\textit{Errors compound.} You're stacking three imperfect steps: SFT introduces biases, reward modeling adds noise, and RL amplifies whatever mistakes survived. Small issues become big ones.

Researchers have tackled each of these:

\textbf{Let the model supervise itself:} \citet{bai2022constitutional} taught models a set of principles, then had them critique and revise their own outputs in a loop. Harmful responses dropped substantially across their test suite, and the human annotators barely had to lift a finger.

\textbf{Let AI do the ranking:} \citet{lee2023rlaif} showed you can swap human annotators for a capable LLM and still get good alignment---at least for summarization. Annotation costs fell by more than 10$\times$ with no obvious quality hit.

\textbf{Borrow from behavioral economics:} \citet{ethayarajh2024kto} noticed that people feel losses more sharply than equivalent gains (classic prospect theory stuff). Their KTO loss function bakes this asymmetry in directly, and they got comparable results using 2--4$\times$ less training data.

\section{Taxonomy of Agentic RL}
\label{sec:taxonomy}

Building on recent surveys \cite{wang2025landscape}, we organize agentic RL research along two axes: the cognitive capability being optimized and the environment where it's applied. This grid exposes recurring themes as well as domain-specific headaches.

\subsection{Core Agentic Capabilities}
Each capability represents a different optimization target:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Hierarchical Goal Decomposition:} Training agents to break complex problems into manageable sub-problems, whether through lookahead planning or recursive planning with reflection.
    \item \textbf{Tool Mastery:} Developing competence in using APIs---selecting the right ones, constructing valid calls, and interpreting results---through trajectory-level feedback.
    \item \textbf{Memory Management:} Breaking free from rigid context limits through learned memory operations---deciding what to stash, when to fetch, and how to compress history without losing the important bits.
    \item \textbf{Deliberate Reasoning:} Reinforcing explicit multi-step thinking (chains, trees, graphs) with process rewards that value valid intermediate deduction steps, not just final answers.
    \item \textbf{Self-Improvement:} Enabling agents to learn new skills without external curriculum---through self-play, curiosity signals, or synthetic task generation.
    \item \textbf{Active Perception:} For vision-equipped agents, learning where to look, when to capture screenshots, and how to balance observation cost against information gain.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Task Domains}
Agents specialize for different environments:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Software Engineering:} Agents working in codebases---navigating repositories, diagnosing bugs from stack traces, writing patches, and validating fixes through tests. SWE-bench exemplifies these challenges.
    \item \textbf{Mathematics:} From word problems and competition math to formal proofs in theorem provers like Lean or Coq.
    \item \textbf{Digital Interfaces:} Controlling graphical interfaces---web browsers, mobile apps, desktop OS---where observation spaces explode and actions require pixel-perfect precision.
    \item \textbf{Physical Interaction:} Connecting language understanding with motor control for robotics and spatial navigation, where simulation-to-reality transfer remains challenging.
    \item \textbf{Agent Networks:} Optimizing how multiple language-model agents coordinate---whether cooperating on shared goals, competing for resources, or negotiating with incomplete information.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Comprehensive RL Taxonomy}
We organize papers into 19 categories not by convenience but by what they optimize. The four-cluster organization reflects fundamental divisions in how agents learn. Core algorithms differ by their optimization target: policy-based methods optimize action selection directly, value-based methods optimize state evaluations, model-based methods learn environment dynamics. Learning paradigms differ by their data source: RLHF uses human preferences, imitation uses demonstrations, offline RL uses logged data. These categories aren't just labels—they reflect different assumptions about what's learnable and how to learn it.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    cluster/.style={rectangle, draw, fill=gray!10, rounded corners, minimum width=4cm, minimum height=2.6cm},
    cat/.style={rectangle, draw, fill=white, text width=3.2cm, text centered, font=\tiny, rounded corners, minimum height=0.35cm},
    title/.style={font=\scriptsize\bfseries},
    scale=0.85, transform shape
]
    % Cluster 1: Core Algorithms (top left)
    \node[cluster] (c1) at (0,4.5) {};
    \node[title] at (0,5.6) {Core Algorithms};
    \node[cat, fill=blue!15] at (0,5) {1. Policy-Based};
    \node[cat, fill=blue!15] at (0,4.5) {2. Value-Based};
    \node[cat, fill=blue!15] at (0,4) {3. Model-Based};

    % Cluster 2: Learning Paradigms (top right)
    \node[cluster] (c2) at (5.5,4.5) {};
    \node[title] at (5.5,5.6) {Learning Paradigms};
    \node[cat, fill=green!15] at (5.5,5) {4. RLHF \& Alignment};
    \node[cat, fill=green!15] at (5.5,4.5) {5. Imitation Learning};
    \node[cat, fill=green!15] at (5.5,4) {6. Offline RL};

    % Cluster 3: Advanced Methods (middle left)
    \node[cluster, minimum height=3.2cm] (c3) at (0,1.2) {};
    \node[title] at (0,2.6) {Advanced Methods};
    \node[cat, fill=orange!15] at (0,2.1) {7. Multi-Agent RL};
    \node[cat, fill=orange!15] at (0,1.6) {8. Hierarchical RL};
    \node[cat, fill=orange!15] at (0,1.1) {9. Meta-Learning};
    \node[cat, fill=orange!15] at (0,0.6) {10. Safe RL};
    \node[cat, fill=orange!15] at (0,0.1) {11. Exploration};

    % Cluster 4: Applications (middle right)
    \node[cluster, minimum height=3.2cm] (c4) at (5.5,1.2) {};
    \node[title] at (5.5,2.6) {Applications};
    \node[cat, fill=purple!15] at (5.5,2.1) {12. Agentic RL};
    \node[cat, fill=purple!15] at (5.5,1.6) {13. Robotics};
    \node[cat, fill=purple!15] at (5.5,1.1) {14. Reasoning};
    \node[cat, fill=purple!15] at (5.5,0.6) {15. Security};
    \node[cat, fill=purple!15] at (5.5,0.1) {16. Benchmarks};

    % Infrastructure cluster (bottom)
    \node[cluster, minimum width=9.5cm, minimum height=1.1cm] (c5) at (2.75,-1.5) {};
    \node[title] at (2.75,-1) {Infrastructure \& Meta};
    \node[cat, fill=cyan!15] at (0,-1.5) {17. Frameworks};
    \node[cat, fill=cyan!15] at (2.75,-1.5) {18. Surveys};
    \node[cat, fill=cyan!15] at (5.5,-1.5) {19. Open-Ended};

    % Arrows - direct vertical connections (no overlap)
    \draw[->, thick, gray!70] (c1.south) -- (c3.north);
    \draw[->, thick, gray!70] (c2.south) -- (c4.north);
    \draw[->, thick, gray!70] (c3.south) -- ++(0,-0.15) -| ([xshift=-1.5cm]c5.north);
    \draw[->, thick, gray!70] (c4.south) -- ++(0,-0.15) -| ([xshift=1.5cm]c5.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Taxonomy of 19 RL research categories organized into five clusters: \textbf{Core Algorithms} (policy/value/model-based methods), \textbf{Learning Paradigms} (how agents acquire behavior), \textbf{Advanced Methods} (multi-agent, hierarchical, safe exploration), \textbf{Applications} (domain-specific implementations), and \textbf{Infrastructure} (frameworks, benchmarks, surveys). Arrows indicate foundational dependencies.}
\label{fig:rl_taxonomy}
\source{Author's synthesis based on surveyed literature}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{1. Core Methods}
At its heart, RL is about credit assignment: figuring out which past action caused the current outcome. Core methods solve the basic loop of trial, error, and feedback---whether estimating state values (Value-Based) or directly optimizing action probabilities (Policy-Based). Figure~\ref{fig:core_methods} sketches the two main families.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={rectangle, draw, text width=2.8cm, text centered, rounded corners, minimum height=0.8cm, font=\scriptsize},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth, thick},
    scale=0.95, transform shape
]
    % Value-Based side
    \node[box, fill=blue!20] (vb_title) at (0,3) {\textbf{Value-Based}};
    \node[box, fill=blue!10] (state) at (0,2) {State $s$};
    \node[box, fill=blue!10] (qfunc) at (0,1) {$Q(s,a)$ Network};
    \node[box, fill=blue!10] (argmax) at (0,0) {$\arg\max_a Q(s,a)$};
    \node[box, fill=blue!10] (action_v) at (0,-1) {Action $a$};

    \draw[arrow] (state) -- (qfunc);
    \draw[arrow] (qfunc) -- (argmax);
    \draw[arrow] (argmax) -- (action_v);

    % Policy-Based side
    \node[box, fill=green!20] (pb_title) at (5,3) {\textbf{Policy-Based}};
    \node[box, fill=green!10] (state_p) at (5,2) {State $s$};
    \node[box, fill=green!10] (policy) at (5,1) {$\pi_\theta(a|s)$};
    \node[box, fill=green!10] (sample) at (5,0) {Sample $a \sim \pi$};
    \node[box, fill=green!10] (action_p) at (5,-1) {Action $a$};

    \draw[arrow] (state_p) -- (policy);
    \draw[arrow] (policy) -- (sample);
    \draw[arrow] (sample) -- (action_p);

    % Examples
    \node[font=\tiny, text width=2.5cm, align=center] at (0,-1.8) {DQN, Rainbow,\\Distributional RL};
    \node[font=\tiny, text width=2.5cm, align=center] at (5,-1.8) {PPO, SAC,\\REINFORCE};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Value-based methods learn $Q(s,a)$ and derive policy via $\arg\max$; policy-based methods directly parameterize $\pi_\theta(a|s)$ and sample actions stochastically.}
\label{fig:core_methods}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{2. RLHF \& Alignment}
Optimization needs a clear objective, but human intent is too complex to write as math equations. Alignment techniques treat human preference as ground truth. Instead of hard-coding rewards, we approximate what humans want by training models to predict human preferences, allowing optimization for fuzzy concepts like "helpfulness."

\subsubsection*{3. Multi-Agent RL}
No single entity can handle everything. Multi-agent systems tackle complex problems by distributing work across specialized roles. The challenge shifts from individual optimization to finding equilibrium strategies (like Nash Equilibrium) where multiple optimizers co-adapt---mirroring how human organizations trade coordination costs for total capability.

\subsubsection*{4. Hierarchical RL}
Complex tasks suffer from the ``curse of dimensionality'' over time. Planning a trip needs high-level decisions (destination) and low-level execution (stepping on the gas). Hierarchical RL abstracts time: a ``manager'' sets abstract goals over long horizons while ``workers'' execute atomic actions to achieve them, reducing the search space for long tasks. Figure~\ref{fig:hierarchical} sketches this two-tier setup.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={rectangle, draw, text width=2.2cm, text centered, rounded corners, minimum height=0.6cm, font=\scriptsize},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth, thick},
    scale=0.9, transform shape
]
    % High level (top row)
    \node[box, fill=purple!30] (manager) at (0,2) {Manager\\$\pi_{high}(g|s)$};
    \node[box, fill=purple!10] (goal) at (3.5,2) {Subgoal $g$};
    \node[box, fill=gray!20] (env) at (7,2) {Environment};

    % Low level (bottom row)
    \node[box, fill=blue!30] (worker) at (3.5,0.5) {Worker\\$\pi_{low}(a|s,g)$};
    \node[box, fill=blue!10] (action) at (7,0.5) {Primitive\\Action $a$};

    % Forward flow arrows
    \draw[arrow] (manager) -- node[above, font=\tiny] {every $k$ steps} (goal);
    \draw[arrow] (goal) -- (worker);
    \draw[arrow] (worker) -- node[above, font=\tiny] {every step} (action);
    \draw[arrow] (action) -- (env);
    
    % Feedback arrow - routed below to avoid crossing boxes
    \draw[arrow, dashed] (env.north) -- ++(0,0.5) -| node[pos=0.25, above, font=\tiny] {reward $r$} (manager.north);

    % Examples
    \node[font=\tiny, text width=5cm, align=center] at (3.5,-0.6) {Options Framework, HAM, HIRO, Feudal Networks};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Hierarchical RL: manager policy selects subgoals every $k$ steps; worker policy executes primitive actions to achieve them. Temporal abstraction reduces effective horizon.}
\label{fig:hierarchical}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{5. Safe \& Constrained RL}
Standard RL assumes maximizing reward is the only goal, but in reality, the path matters. Safe RL introduces constrained optimization---agents must maximize utility while staying within safety boundaries. This stops the agent from gaming the objective via risky or unacceptable shortcuts.

\subsubsection*{6. Curiosity \& Exploration}
In environments with sparse feedback, reward-driven agents learn nothing because they never stumble upon success. Exploration methods inject an intrinsic drive---optimizing not just for external reward but for shrinking uncertainty or chasing novelty. This lets agents piece together world models even absent explicit tasks. Figure~\ref{fig:exploration} shows how intrinsic rewards patch the sparse-feedback problem.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={rectangle, draw, text width=2cm, text centered, rounded corners, minimum height=0.6cm, font=\scriptsize},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth, thick},
    scale=0.9, transform shape
]
    % Agent
    \node[box, fill=blue!20] (agent) at (0,0) {Agent};
    \node[box, fill=gray!20] (env) at (3,0) {Environment};

    % Rewards
    \node[box, fill=red!20] (ext) at (6,0.5) {Extrinsic $r_e$\\{\tiny(sparse)}};
    \node[box, fill=green!20] (int) at (6,-0.5) {Intrinsic $r_i$\\{\tiny(curiosity)}};
    \node[box, fill=orange!20] (total) at (9,0) {$r = r_e + \beta r_i$};

    \draw[arrow] (agent) -- node[above, font=\tiny] {action} (env);
    \draw[arrow] (env) -- (ext);
    \draw[arrow] (env) -- (int);
    \draw[arrow] (ext) -- (total);
    \draw[arrow] (int) -- (total);
    \draw[arrow] (total.south) -- ++(0,-0.8) -| node[near end, below, font=\tiny] {learn} (agent.south);

    % Methods
    \node[font=\tiny, text width=5cm, align=center] at (4.5,-1.5) {RND: prediction error | ICM: forward model error | Go-Explore: state archive};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Exploration via intrinsic motivation: curiosity rewards ($r_i$) supplement sparse external rewards ($r_e$), enabling learning in hard-exploration domains.}
\label{fig:exploration}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{7. Model-Based RL}
Trial-and-error in the real world is slow and expensive. Model-based methods learn environment dynamics, allowing agents to ``dream'' or simulate futures without acting. By shifting computation from physical interaction to internal simulation, agents squeeze far more learning out of each interaction---swapping real-world trials for simulated ones. Figure~\ref{fig:model_based} shows the model-based learning loop.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={rectangle, draw, text width=2.2cm, text centered, rounded corners, minimum height=0.7cm, font=\scriptsize},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth, thick},
    scale=0.9, transform shape
]
    % Real world
    \node[box, fill=red!20] (real) at (0,0) {Real World};
    \node[box, fill=orange!20] (data) at (3,0) {Experience\\$(s,a,r,s')$};
    \node[box, fill=yellow!20] (model) at (6,0) {Learned Model\\$\hat{T}(s'|s,a)$};

    % Imagination
    \node[box, fill=cyan!20] (imagine) at (6,-1.5) {Imagined\\Rollouts};
    \node[box, fill=green!20] (plan) at (3,-1.5) {Planning /\\Policy Update};
    \node[box, fill=blue!20] (policy) at (0,-1.5) {Improved\\Policy $\pi$};

    \draw[arrow] (real) -- node[above, font=\tiny] {interact} (data);
    \draw[arrow] (data) -- node[above, font=\tiny] {train} (model);
    \draw[arrow] (model) -- (imagine);
    \draw[arrow] (imagine) -- node[below, font=\tiny] {simulate} (plan);
    \draw[arrow] (plan) -- (policy);
    \draw[arrow] (policy) -- (real);

    % Label
    \node[font=\tiny, text width=4cm, align=center] at (3,-2.5) {World Models, Dreamer, MuZero};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Model-based RL loop: learn dynamics model from real experience, then use imagined rollouts for planning, cutting data requirements 10--100$\times$.}
\label{fig:model_based}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{8. Imitation Learning}
Searching from scratch is inefficient when solutions already exist. Imitation learning treats expert behavior as a strong prior, narrowing the search to "proven effective strategies." This bootstraps learning, avoiding rediscovery of basic competencies.

\subsubsection*{9. Agentic RL}
This applies control theory to language and digital tools. Unlike physical control, the "action space" involves generating tokens or API calls, and the "state space" is the context window. The twist: reasoning is no longer static output---it is a latent policy that pokes at the digital environment until the answer clicks into place.

\subsubsection*{10. Offline RL}
Online interaction is often impossible due to safety or cost (medical treatment, power grids). Offline RL tackles the counterfactual problem: learning solid policies from frozen historical data. The hard part is staying conservative---avoiding actions that look good but lead to unseen states.

\subsubsection*{11. Meta \& Continual RL}
Standard models are brittle---they master one task but fail if rules change slightly. Meta-learning shifts the target from ``performance on Task A'' to ``speed of adaptation to Task B.'' It optimizes the learning algorithm itself, creating agents that are not just skilled but adaptable to changing environments. Figure~\ref{fig:meta_rl} shows the meta-learning loop.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    box/.style={rectangle, draw, text width=2.4cm, text centered, rounded corners, minimum height=0.6cm, font=\scriptsize},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth, thick},
    scale=0.85, transform shape
]
    % Meta level (left)
    \node[box, fill=red!20, minimum height=1.8cm] (meta) at (0,1) {Meta-Learner\\$\theta$};

    % Task distribution (middle-left) - stacked vertically
    \node[box, fill=orange!20] (task1) at (3.5,2) {Task 1};
    \node[box, fill=orange!20] (task2) at (3.5,1) {Task 2};
    \node[box, fill=orange!20] (taskn) at (3.5,0) {Task $n$};

    % Adapted parameters (middle-right)
    \node[box, fill=green!20, minimum height=1.8cm] (adapt) at (7,1) {Adapted\\$\theta'_i$};

    % Evaluation (right)
    \node[box, fill=blue!20, minimum height=1.8cm] (eval) at (10.2,1) {Fast\\Adaptation};

    % Forward arrows - fan out from meta to tasks
    \draw[arrow] (meta.east) -- (task1.west);
    \draw[arrow] (meta.east) -- (task2.west);
    \draw[arrow] (meta.east) -- (taskn.west);
    
    % Fan in from tasks to adapted
    \draw[arrow] (task1.east) -- (adapt.west);
    \draw[arrow] (task2.east) -- (adapt.west);
    \draw[arrow] (taskn.east) -- (adapt.west);
    
    % Forward to evaluation
    \draw[arrow] (adapt) -- node[above, font=\tiny] {few shots} (eval);

    % Feedback loop - routed below with clear path
    \draw[arrow, dashed, gray] (eval.south) -- ++(0,-0.8) -| node[pos=0.75, below, font=\tiny] {meta-gradient} (meta.south);

    % Method examples
    \node[font=\tiny, text width=5cm, align=center] at (5.1,-1.2) {MAML, RL$^2$, PEARL};
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{Meta-RL: learn initialization $\theta$ that enables fast adaptation to new tasks with few gradient steps. Outer loop optimizes ``learning to learn.''}
\label{fig:meta_rl}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection*{12. Open-Ended Learning \& Generalization}
Fixed benchmarks lead to overfitting. Open-ended learning mimics evolution, where environment and agent co-evolve. The system generates its own curriculum of increasingly difficult tasks, ensuring capabilities must constantly expand. The goal isn't solving a task but indefinitely increasing solvable complexity.

\subsubsection*{13. Systems \& Modular RL}
The bottleneck in modern AI is often data throughput, not algorithms. Systems RL tackles the engineering physics of training: moving massive experience data from environments to learners efficiently. It treats the training loop as a high-performance distributed computing problem.

\subsubsection*{14. Multi-Objective RL}
Real problems rarely have a single success metric. Agents must balance speed against accuracy, or profit against risk. Multi-objective RL rejects scalar rewards, instead solving for the Pareto frontier---all optimal trade-offs. This allows dynamic selection between conflicting goals at runtime.

\subsubsection*{15. Embodied \& Robotics RL}
Intelligence needs grounding in physical reality. Embodied RL deals with noisy, continuous, unforgiving physics. The hard part is the ``sim-to-real'' gap: learning policies in perfect simulation that survive real-world friction, latency, and sensor noise.

\subsubsection*{16. Surveys \& Overviews}
Science needs consolidation. As research explodes, these works serve as map-makers, synthesizing scattered discoveries into coherent frameworks. They chart what we know, spotlight blind spots, and stake out the questions that will drive the next wave of work.

\subsubsection*{17. Reasoning \& Search}
Standard LLMs use "System 1" thinking---fast, heuristic pattern matching. Reasoning RL adds "System 2" capabilities by treating thought generation as a search problem. Models learn to navigate reasoning trees, backtracking and verifying, turning compute time into logical accuracy.

\subsubsection*{18. LLM Security \& Red Teaming}
Complex systems have hidden failure modes. Red teaming applies adversarial optimization to find these weaknesses. By treating attackers as RL agents trying to maximize target model failure, we automatically discover and patch vulnerabilities humans might never think to test.

\subsubsection*{19. Benchmarks \& Evaluations}
Progress demands measurement. Without agreed-upon yardsticks, we cannot distinguish genuine advances from cherry-picked results. Good benchmarks act as the community's shared exam---forcing models to prove their worth on problems they have not memorized.

\section{Policy Gradient Methods: PPO and TRPO}
Policy optimization has a cliff-edge problem: a single bad update can destroy the policy, and because the data distribution depends on the policy, it can never recover. PPO solves this by enforcing a "trust region"—a mathematical guardrail ensuring we never step too far into the unknown. Trust-region methods prevent catastrophic performance degradation by limiting policy updates. PPO \cite{schulman2017proximal} makes this practical with a clipping mechanism:
\begin{equation}
L^{CLIP}(\theta) = \mathbb{E}_t \left[ \min(r_t(\theta) \hat{A}_t, \text{clip}(r_t(\theta), 1-\epsilon, 1+\epsilon) \hat{A}_t) \right]
\end{equation}
where $r_t(\theta)$ is the probability ratio between new and old policies, and $\hat{A}_t$ estimates advantage. The clipping threshold $\epsilon$ (typically 0.1-0.2) bounds how much the policy can change per iteration. Despite proven stability, PPO needs lots of memory---maintaining actor, critic, reference, and reward models---and is sensitive to learning rate, clipping range, and advantage estimation.

\begin{tcolorbox}[colback=gray!5!white, colframe=gray!60!black, title=\textbf{Worked Example: PPO Clipping in Action}]
\small
Suppose $\epsilon = 0.2$ and the advantage $\hat{A}_t = +3$ (a good action). The probability ratio $r_t(\theta)$ measures how much more likely the new policy makes this action:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lccl}
\toprule
\textbf{Scenario} & $r_t(\theta)$ & \textbf{Clipped?} & \textbf{Gradient Effect} \\
\midrule
Small update & 1.1 & No (in $[0.8, 1.2]$) & $1.1 \times 3 = 3.3$ \\
Large update & 1.5 & Yes $\rightarrow$ 1.2 & $1.2 \times 3 = 3.6$ (capped) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Key insight:} Without clipping, the large update would give gradient $1.5 \times 3 = 4.5$, encouraging even bigger jumps. Clipping caps the benefit at $3.6$, preventing the policy from changing too fast and destabilizing training.
\end{tcolorbox}

\section{DPO: Skip the Reward Model Entirely}
\label{sec:dpo}
\textbf{Transition:} PPO's multi-model architecture works but creates engineering headaches. What if we could eliminate the reward model entirely?

Building an independent reward model is not always necessary if the objective can be solved algebraically. The authors of the DPO framework \cite{rafailov2023direct} demonstrated that the RLHF objective can be restructured until the reward model cancels out. What's left is a loss you can calculate based on pairs of preferences---no intermediate scoring step:
\begin{equation}
L_{\text{DPO}} = -\mathbb{E}_{(x,y_w,y_l)} \left[ \log \sigma \left( \beta \log \frac{\pi_\theta(y_w|x)}{\pi_{\text{ref}}(y_w|x)} - \beta \log \frac{\pi_\theta(y_l|x)}{\pi_{\text{ref}}(y_l|x)} \right) \right]
\end{equation}
Here $y_w$ is the response annotators liked better and $y_l$ is the one they didn't; $\beta$ controls how aggressively you push toward preferred responses (crank it too high and you'll overfit to idiosyncrasies in your preference data).

The appeal is obvious: simpler pipeline, fewer moving parts, less stuff that can break. The downside is equally clear---you're locked into whatever preferences you collected at the start. DPO can't explore; it just learns to mimic the patterns in your dataset. For tasks where the optimal strategy isn't well-represented in training, that's a real limitation.

\begin{tcolorbox}[colback=gray!5!white, colframe=gray!60!black, title=\textbf{Worked Example: DPO $\beta$ Parameter Tuning}]
\small
The $\beta$ parameter controls preference strength. Consider a prompt where annotators preferred response A over B:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{lccc}
\toprule
$\beta$ & \textbf{Effect} & \textbf{Risk} & \textbf{Use Case} \\
\midrule
0.1 (low) & Weak preference signal & Slow convergence & Noisy/inconsistent labels \\
0.5 (typical) & Balanced learning & --- & Most applications \\
1.0+ (high) & Strong preference push & Overfitting to quirks & Clean, consistent data \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{For practical implementation:} Start with $\beta = 0.1$--$0.5$.
 If the model ignores preferences, increase $\beta$. If it becomes repetitive or loses fluency, decrease $\beta$. Monitor KL divergence from the reference model---if it spikes, $\beta$ is too high.
\end{tcolorbox}

\section{GRPO: The Memory-Efficient Alternative That Took Over}
\label{sec:grpo}
\textbf{Transition:} DPO simplifies training but sacrifices exploration. GRPO takes a different path---keeping online learning while dramatically reducing memory requirements.

Standard RL uses a ``critic'' network to estimate how good a state is, which helps reduce variance in gradient estimates. But here's the thing: you don't actually need to know how good something is in absolute terms---you just need to know whether it's better or worse than the alternatives. The DeepSeek-R1 team \cite{deepseek2025r1} ran with this idea and stripped out the critic entirely. Their approach, GRPO, works like this: create multiple outputs for a given query, score them all
 (using whatever reward signal you have---test cases, a learned model, even just rule-based checks), then rank them within the batch. Responses that beat the batch average get reinforced; responses below average get penalized. That's it.

The implications for memory usage are significant. PPO needs to keep four models in VRAM: the policy, a reference copy, a critic, and often a reward model. GRPO eliminates the critic entirely, requiring only the policy and a reference model for the KL penalty (2$\times$ vs 4$\times$ model size), plus a scoring function. For a 70B parameter model, that's a 50\% memory reduction that can make the difference between needing multiple nodes and fitting on a single machine.

Did it work? DeepSeek-R1 hit 79.8\% on AIME 2024 math competition problems \cite{deepseek2025r1}, putting it in the same ballpark as human medalists. And they did it without the infrastructure burden that made PPO-style training prohibitively expensive for most labs.

\subsection{The GRPO Family Tree}
Once GRPO proved itself, people started tweaking it for specific use cases:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Flow-GRPO} \cite{li2025flowgrpo}: Handles multi-turn conversations by computing advantages over entire dialogue trajectories, not just single responses. Helps agents maintain coherence across long exchanges instead of optimizing each reply in isolation.

    \item \textbf{M-GRPO} \cite{hong2025mgrpo}: Built for multi-agent setups where a ``manager'' delegates subtasks to ``workers.'' Each level in the hierarchy keeps its own baseline, so agents can specialize without fighting over a shared reward signal.

    \item \textbf{GRPO-TCR:} Adds a penalty for malformed tool calls. Generate an invalid API invocation and your advantage score takes a hit---a simple way to teach syntactic correctness without separate supervised training.

    \item \textbf{FoldGRPO:} Adjusts the KL penalty on the fly. If the policy is changing too fast, tighten the leash; if training is stable, loosen it. This adaptive scheduling helps with the catastrophic forgetting problem in long-horizon tasks.
\end{itemize}

\noindent\textbf{Novel PPO Variants:} Parallel developments have improved PPO for agentic settings:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{DAPO (Decoupled Clip and Dynamic Sampling Policy Optimization)} \cite{bytedance2025dapo}: Removes the clipping mechanism and introduces dynamic sampling to address the entropy collapse problem in large-scale RL training. Achieves 50 points on AIME 2024 with Qwen2.5-32B, providing a fully open-source alternative to proprietary reasoning systems.
\end{itemize}

\noindent\textbf{Additional Novel Algorithms (2024--2025):} Beyond GRPO and PPO variants, new algorithms have emerged:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Tree-GRPO} \cite{ji2025treegrpo}: Applies tree search to LLM agent reinforcement learning, outperforming chain-based approaches over a selection of 11 datasets and 3 QA task types.
 Multi-child branching enables multiple policy updates per forward pass with fine-grained credit assignment via reward backpropagation through the tree structure.

    \item \textbf{ReMax} \cite{li2024remax}: Simplifies GRPO further by using maximum-likelihood estimation on the best response in each group, eliminating negative gradients entirely and improving stability.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Algorithm Distribution in Current Research}
Tallying up \methodN\ method variants extracted from our surveyed papers, the algorithm breakdown shakes out like this: \textbf{GRPO} and its variants dominate with 31 variants (39\%), followed by \textbf{PPO} variants with 18 variants (22\%), \textbf{DPO} derivatives with 8 variants (10\%), and \textbf{custom/novel methods} comprising 23 variants (29\%). The numbers track the field's drift toward RAM-friendly pipelines that keep everything in one model.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering

\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{@{}lcc>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}X@{}}

\midrule

DPO/Derivatives & 8 & 10\% & Zephyr, STEP-DPO, HRPO, Preference-DPO \\

\noindent\textbf{Architecture Paradigms:} The studies cluster around three dominant architectural patterns:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Modular Architectures (52\%):} Separate components for perception, planning, and execution---allowing independent optimization and interpretability.
    \item \textbf{End-to-End Architectures (28\%):} Unified models processing raw observations directly to actions---maximizing integration but requiring more data.
    \item \textbf{Hierarchical Architectures (20\%):} Multi-level policies with manager-worker dynamics---balancing abstraction with fine-grained control.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Comparative Analysis of Alignment Algorithms}
Table~\ref{tab:algorithm_comparison} lines up the three main alignment algorithms across the dimensions that matter for agentic AI work.
