\section*{Core Experimental Suite}
\label{sec:comprehensive_experiments}

This section presents five focused experiments addressing the major research directions identified in our survey. Each experiment isolates a fundamental question with minimal conditions to maximize interpretability on academic compute budgets.

\subsection{Experiment 1: Algorithm Comparison (PPO vs DPO vs GRPO)}
\textbf{Core Question:} Which RL algorithm achieves the best sample efficiency and final performance on agentic tasks?

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item E1.1: PPO with learned reward model (standard RLHF pipeline)
    \item E1.2: DPO with preference pairs (without a separate reward network)
    \item E1.3: GRPO with group-relative advantages (no critic network)
    \item E1.4: SFT-only baseline (no RL fine-tuning)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Benchmarks:} WebArena-Lite (web navigation), SWE-bench Lite (code repair), MATH subset (reasoning).

\textbf{Metrics:} Task success rate (TSR), sample efficiency ($I_{80}$), training stability (variance across seeds), memory footprint.

\textbf{Rationale:} This experiment directly tests H1 (GRPO sample efficiency) and provides the foundational comparison that informs all subsequent experiments. Literature suggests GRPO should converge faster due to lower memory overhead allowing for increased batch sizes.

\subsection{Experiment 2: Tool Use and Grounding}
\textbf{Core Question:} Does RL training reduce tool hallucination compared to SFT, and can agents discover tool use without demonstrations?

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item E2.1: SFT on tool-use demonstrations only
    \item E2.2: SFT + GRPO with outcome rewards (tool available but not demonstrated)
    \item E2.3: GRPO from scratch with tools (no SFT warmup)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Benchmarks:} ToolBench API tasks, WebArena tool-heavy subset.

\textbf{Metrics:} Valid API call rate, hallucination rate (calls to non-existent APIs), task success, episodes to first successful tool use.

\textbf{Rationale:} Tests H2 (tool-use generalization). RL optimizes for outcomes---invalid calls yield zero reward---so should naturally suppress hallucinations. If E2.3 discovers tool use autonomously, it unlocks tool learning for domains without demonstration data.

\subsection{Experiment 3: Memory Architecture (RAG vs Extended Context)}
\textbf{Core Question:} For long-horizon agentic tasks, is retrieval-augmented memory more efficient than extended context windows?

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item E3.1: 8K context window (baseline)
    \item E3.2: 32K context window (extended)
    \item E3.3: 8K context + RAG retrieval (top-5 passages per step)
    \item E3.4: 8K context + RAG with adaptive retrieval threshold
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Benchmarks:} Multi-step WebArena tasks (50+ actions), documentation-heavy SWE-bench tasks.

\textbf{Metrics:} Task success on 100+ step tasks, compute per step (FLOPs), memory fidelity (recall of information from early steps), retrieval precision.

\textbf{Rationale:} Tests H3 (RAG conditional benefit). Attention scales quadratically; retrieval is constant-cost. RAG should win on efficiency but may lose on precision. The crossover point reveals when each approach dominates.

\subsection{Experiment 4: Multi-Agent vs Single-Agent}
\textbf{Core Question:} Does task decomposition across specialized agents outperform a single agent with equivalent compute budget?

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item E4.1: Single agent, full episode budget
    \item E4.2: Two agents (planner + executor), shared budget
    \item E4.3: Three agents (planner + coder + reviewer), shared budget
    \item E4.4: Hierarchical manager-worker topology (M-GRPO style)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Benchmarks:} SWE-bench (code repair benefits from review), complex WebArena tasks (booking flows).

\textbf{Metrics:} Success per unit compute, coordination overhead (tokens spent on inter-agent communication), failure mode analysis (coordination breakdown vs. single-agent confusion).

\textbf{Rationale:} Multi-agent systems add complexity. This experiment determines whether coordination overhead is justified by improved task decomposition, or whether single-agent scaling is more efficient for current benchmarks.

\subsection{Experiment 5: Scaling Efficiency (Test-Time Compute vs Model Size)}
\textbf{Core Question:} Is inference-time reasoning (sampling + voting) more compute-efficient than training larger models?

\textbf{Design:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item E5.1: 8B model, single generation
    \item E5.2: 8B model, best-of-16 sampling with majority vote
    \item E5.3: 8B model, best-of-16 with process reward model reranking
    \item E5.4: 70B model, single generation (if compute permits)
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Benchmarks:} MATH subset, AIME-style problems, reasoning-heavy WebArena tasks.

\textbf{Metrics:} Accuracy per FLOP (training + inference), latency, accuracy ceiling.

\textbf{Rationale:} Recent work \cite{snell2024scaling} shows test-time compute can match larger models. This experiment quantifies the tradeoff for agentic tasks specifically, where reasoning chains are longer and sampling diversity may matter more.

\section*{Summary of Experimental Philosophy}

These five experiments collectively address the core questions from our literature survey: algorithm selection (Exp 1), tool integration (Exp 2), memory architecture (Exp 3), coordination patterns (Exp 4), and scaling strategies (Exp 5). Total experimental runs: 19 conditions across 5 experiments, each with 3 random seeds = 57 runs.

The goal is not to achieve state-of-the-art numbers---that requires proprietary compute and data. The goal is to determine which architectural choices and training strategies are \textit{necessary} for capable agents versus which are premature optimization.

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Stage 1 (Cold Start):} SFT on high-quality demonstrations (ReAct style). This teaches the model basic tool-use syntax and reasoning formats.
    \item \textbf{Stage 2 (RL Fine-Tuning):} Apply PPO/GRPO with either learned scorers or step-by-step verification. PPO runs standard RLHF with trained rewards---stable but heavy on compute. DPO sidesteps the explicit scorer, optimizing straight from preferences. GRPO shows promise for handling group-level signals minus the critic overhead.
    \item \textbf{Stage 3 (RAG Integration):} Augment the fine-tuned agent with retrieval for documentation and memory. We plug in retrieval mechanisms to let agents access external knowledge bases dynamically.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Infrastructure and Tools}
All experiments ran on the \textbf{Tinker RL platform} using the \textbf{Atropos} GRPO training framework, which provides cloud GPU access via API without requiring local hardware. This setup enabled rapid iteration across model families and scales.

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Compute:} Tinker cloud GPUs accessed via Atropos API (no local GPU required).
    \item \textbf{Fine-tuning:} LoRA (Low-Rank Adaptation) with rank 32, targeting attention and MLP projections. Only $\sim$0.1\% of total parameters modified.
    \item \textbf{Training:} GRPO with group size 16 (16 completions sampled per question), learning rate $4 \times 10^{-5}$, binary reward signal (format + correctness).
    \item \textbf{Checkpointing:} Automatic checkpoint saving every training step via Atropos; training state recoverable from any checkpoint.
    \item \textbf{Code:} All experiment configs at \texttt{pes-llm-research/tinker-rl-lab/atropos/configs/}; notebooks at \texttt{atropos/notebooks/}.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Reproducibility Protocol}
Reproducibility is not about listing procedures—it's about ensuring results aren't artifacts. Fixed seeds (42, 123, 456) across three independent runs test whether results depend on initialization or are robust to randomness. Hyperparameters aren't arbitrary: learning rate $2 \times 10^{-5}$ is small enough for stable updates but large enough for reasonable convergence speed. Batch size 8 reflects GPU memory constraints—smaller batches introduce more noise, larger require distributed training. The warmup ratio 0.03 prevents early instabilities. RL-specific parameters aren't magic numbers: PPO clip ratio 0.2 balances exploration and exploitation based on empirical stability studies. DPO $\beta = 0.1$ controls the KL penalty strength based on preference noise estimates. GRPO group size 8 provides enough samples for reliable advantage estimation. Every parameter reflects a trade-off between statistical efficiency, computational cost, and training stability.

\subsection{Statistical Analysis Framework}
We employ rigorous statistical methods to validate our hypotheses:

\textbf{Primary Metrics:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Task Success Rate (TSR):} Fraction of episodes where the agent achieves the specified goal state.
    \item \textbf{Sample Efficiency ($I_{80}$):} Number of environment interactions required to reach 80\% of final performance at $N_{\max}$.
    \item \textbf{Reasoning Quality:} For tasks with ground-truth reasoning chains, we compute step-level accuracy using process reward models.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Statistical Tests:}
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Algorithm Comparison:} Paired t-tests with Bonferroni correction for multiple comparisons (5 algorithm pairs $\times$ 3 benchmarks = 15 tests, $\alpha = 0.05/15$). We report statistical significance where $p < 0.05$, with 95\% confidence intervals calculated via bootstrapping (1000 resamples).
    \item \textbf{Effect Sizes:} Cohen's $d$ reported for all pairwise comparisons; effects classified as small ($d < 0.2$), medium ($0.2 \leq d < 0.8$), or large ($d \geq 0.8$).
    \item \textbf{Confidence Intervals:} Bootstrap 95\% CIs (1000 resamples) for all point estimates.
    \item \textbf{ANOVA:} Two-way ANOVA to test H4 (algorithm $\times$ task interaction), followed by Tukey HSD post-hoc tests.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Reporting Standards:}
All experiments follow NeurIPS reproducibility guidelines. We report negative results, computational costs (GPU-hours), and failure modes. Code, checkpoints, and evaluation logs will be released upon publication.

\subsection{Baseline Comparisons}
To contextualize our fine-tuned agents, we establish:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Zero-shot:} Base model with task-specific system prompt, no demonstrations.
    \item \textbf{Few-shot (3-shot):} Base model with three in-context examples per task category.
    \item \textbf{Chain-of-Thought:} Few-shot with explicit reasoning traces in demonstrations.
    \item \textbf{SFT-only:} Supervised fine-tuning without RL optimization (Stage 1 only).
    \item \textbf{Published SOTA:} Comparison against reported results from SWE-bench, WebArena, and OSWorld leaderboards.
\end{itemize}

\section{Actual Experimental Results}
\label{sec:actual_results}

We report results from eight GRPO experiments across three model families, three benchmarks, and scales from 0.5B to 30B parameters. All experiments used binary rewards: format correctness (0/1) and answer correctness (0/1), summed to a maximum reward of 2.0.

\subsection{Summary of Results}

\begin{table}[htbp]

\caption{GRPO Experiment Results Summary}
\label{tab:results_summary}
\begin{tabular}{llllll}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Type} & \textbf{Benchmark} & \textbf{Step 0} & \textbf{Final} & \textbf{Convergence} \\
\midrule
Qwen3-8B       & Base          & GSM8K  & 7\%   & \textbf{100\%} & Step $\sim$30 \\
Qwen3-30B MoE  & Base          & GSM8K  & 8\%   & \textbf{99.2\%} & Step $\sim$35 (volatile) \\
Llama-3.1-8B   & Instruct      & GSM8K  & 79\%  & \textbf{100\%} & Step $\sim$35 \\
Llama-3.2-3B   & Base          & GSM8K  & 2\%   & \textbf{2\%}   & \textit{Did not converge} \\
Qwen3-8B       & Base          & MATH   & 3\%   & $\sim$14\%     & Ongoing \\
Llama-3.1-8B   & Instruct      & MATH   & 18\%  & 50--87\%       & Ongoing (volatile) \\
Qwen3-3.8B     & Base          & HumanEval (coding) & 32\% & \textbf{40\%} & Step 50, \$0.50 cost \\

\subsection{GSM8K Results: Training Curves}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}
    \begin{axis}[
        xlabel={Training Step},
        ylabel={Reward (Accuracy)},
        width=13cm,
        height=7.5cm,
        legend pos=south east,
        grid=major,
        ymin=0, ymax=105,
        xmin=0, xmax=40,
    ]
    % Qwen3-8B base: 7% -> 100%, phase transition around step 22-25
    \addplot[color=blue, thick, mark=o, mark size=2pt] coordinates {
        (0,7) (5,9) (10,11) (15,13) (20,18) (22,42) (25,78) (28,95) (30,100) (35,100)
    };
    % Qwen3-30B MoE: volatile but reaches 99%
    \addplot[color=red, thick, mark=triangle, mark size=2pt] coordinates {
        (0,8) (5,12) (10,20) (15,45) (20,68) (25,82) (28,91) (30,85) (32,97) (35,99)
    };
    % Llama-3.1-8B Instruct: starts high at 79%, reaches 100%
    \addplot[color=green!60!black, thick, mark=square, mark size=2pt] coordinates {
        (0,79) (5,83) (10,88) (15,93) (20,96) (25,98) (30,99) (35,100)
    };
    % Llama-3.2-3B base: flat negative result
    \addplot[color=gray, thick, dashed, mark=x, mark size=2pt] coordinates {
        (0,2) (5,2) (10,2) (15,2) (20,2) (25,2) (30,2) (35,2)
    };
    \legend{Qwen3-8B (base), Qwen3-30B MoE, Llama-3.1-8B (instruct), Llama-3.2-3B (base)}
    \end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{GSM8K training curves for all four models. Qwen3-8B (base) demonstrates a characteristic RL emergence/phase transition around steps 22--25, jumping from $\sim$13\% to $\sim$78\% in five steps before saturating at 100\%. Llama-3.2-3B (dashed) is the negative result: reward never exceeds 2\% because the model lacks the minimum capacity to generate any correct solutions, leaving GRPO with no positive signal to learn from.}
\label{fig:gsm8k_curves}
\end{figure}

\subsection{MATH Competition Results}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}
    \begin{axis}[
        xlabel={Training Step},
        ylabel={Reward (Accuracy)},
        width=13cm,
        height=6cm,
        legend pos=north west,
        grid=major,
        ymin=0, ymax=100,
        xmin=0, xmax=30,
    ]
    % Qwen3-8B on MATH: much lower, ~14% at step 25
    \addplot[color=blue, thick, mark=o, mark size=2pt] coordinates {
        (0,3) (5,5) (10,7) (15,9) (20,11) (25,14)
    };
    % Llama-3.1-8B Instruct on MATH: volatile 50-87%
    \addplot[color=green!60!black, thick, mark=square, mark size=2pt] coordinates {
        (0,18) (5,38) (10,55) (15,45) (20,72) (25,87) (27,58) (29,75)
    };
    \legend{Qwen3-8B (base), Llama-3.1-8B (instruct)}
    \end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{MATH competition benchmark results (ongoing). The gap between GSM8K (100\% for Qwen3-8B) and MATH ($\sim$14\%) reveals a substantial difficulty cliff. The instruct model achieves 50--87\% but with high variance, reflecting the heterogeneous difficulty of competition problems (AMC algebra vs.\ AIME geometry).}
\label{fig:math_curves}
\end{figure}

\subsection{HumanEval Coding Results (Madhu)}

Madhu's experiment applied GRPO to code generation using the HumanEval benchmark (164 Python programming problems). The reward signal was binary: 1.0 if the generated code passes all unit tests (executed via subprocess), 0.0 otherwise.

Training Qwen3-3.8B for 50 steps on a single RTX 4090 (Vast.ai, \$0.50 total) improved pass@1 from 32\% to \textbf{40\%} --- an absolute gain of 8 percentage points. This demonstrates that GRPO generalises from arithmetic reasoning to program synthesis: the policy learns to self-correct code by observing which completions satisfy test constraints, without any supervised examples of correct solutions.

\subsection{GRPO Training Pipeline Architecture}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    node distance=1.4cm and 2.2cm,
    box/.style={draw, rounded corners=4pt, minimum width=2.8cm, minimum height=1.1cm,
                text centered, font=\small\sffamily, fill=blue!8, draw=blue!50!black, thick},
    cloud/.style={draw, ellipse, minimum width=2.6cm, minimum height=1.0cm,
                  text centered, font=\small\sffamily, fill=orange!10, draw=orange!60!black, thick},
    arrow/.style={-{Stealth[length=7pt]}, thick, draw=gray!70!black},
    darrow/.style={-{Stealth[length=7pt]}, thick, draw=red!60!black, dashed},
]

% Row 1: Dataset / Prompt pool
\node[cloud] (dataset) {Dataset\\(e.g.\ GSM8K)};

% Row 1: Environment
\node[box, right=of dataset] (env) {Environment\\(Atropos)};

% Row 2: Rollout server (below env)
\node[box, below=of env] (rollout) {Rollout Server\\(vLLM / SGLANG)};

% Row 2: Trainer (right of rollout)
\node[box, right=of rollout] (trainer) {Tinker Trainer\\(GRPO + LoRA)};

% Row 1: Checkpoint store (right of env)
\node[box, right=of env] (ckpt) {Checkpoint\\Store};

% Row 2: Updated policy (below ckpt)
\node[cloud, below=of ckpt] (policy) {Updated\\Policy};

% Arrows
\draw[arrow] (dataset) -- node[above, font=\tiny\sffamily]{prompts} (env);
\draw[arrow] (env) -- node[right, font=\tiny\sffamily]{batch} (rollout);
\draw[arrow] (rollout) -- node[above, font=\tiny\sffamily]{trajectories + scores} (trainer);
\draw[arrow] (trainer) -- node[right, font=\tiny\sffamily]{checkpoint} (policy);
\draw[darrow] (policy) -- node[above, font=\tiny\sffamily]{reload weights} (rollout);
\draw[arrow] (env.north east) to[bend left=18] node[above, font=\tiny\sffamily]{reward signal} (trainer.north west);
\draw[darrow] (ckpt.south) -- (policy.north);
\draw[arrow] (trainer.north) to[bend right=15] node[right, font=\tiny\sffamily]{save} (ckpt.east);

\end{tikzpicture}
\caption{GRPO training pipeline. The Atropos Environment samples prompts from the dataset and forwards batches to the Rollout Server (vLLM), which generates $G$ completions per prompt. The Environment scores each completion (binary reward) and passes trajectory--reward pairs to the Tinker Trainer, which performs the GRPO policy gradient update over LoRA parameters. Updated weights are checkpointed and reloaded into the Rollout Server every $N$ steps for on-policy training.}
\label{fig:grpo_pipeline}
\end{figure}

\section{Statistical Analysis of Experimental Results}
\label{sec:statistical_analysis}

All statistical tests were computed from the step-level mean rewards logged by the Atropos environment (each step mean $= k/128$ where $k$ is the number of correct completions out of $n{=}128$ binary samples: 8 groups $\times$ 16 rollouts).  Tests were implemented in \texttt{tinker\_atropos/stats\_utils.py} and run post-hoc on the full 50-step series for the three completed GSM8K experiments.

\subsection{Bootstrap 95\% Confidence Intervals}

Bootstrap CIs (1000 resamples, seed 42) were computed by pooling the reconstructed binary observations for each window.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Bootstrap 95\% Confidence Intervals for Initial and Final Performance}
\label{tab:bootstrap_ci}
\begin{tabular}{llccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Window} & \textbf{Mean} & \textbf{95\% CI lower} & \textbf{95\% CI upper} \\
\midrule
Qwen3-8B           & First 5 steps   & 0.0453 & 0.0297 & 0.0609 \\
Qwen3-8B           & Last 10 steps   & 0.9437 & 0.9320 & 0.9563 \\
\addlinespace
Llama-3.2-3B       & First 5 steps   & 0.0125 & 0.0047 & 0.0219 \\
Llama-3.2-3B       & Last 10 steps   & 0.0195 & 0.0133 & 0.0273 \\
\addlinespace
Llama-3.1-8B-Instr.& First 5 steps   & 0.7328 & 0.6984 & 0.7672 \\
Llama-3.1-8B-Instr.& Last 10 steps   & 0.9797 & 0.9727 & 0.9875 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The non-overlapping CIs for Qwen3-8B ($[0.030, 0.061]$ initial vs.\ $[0.932, 0.956]$ final) confirm statistically that training drove a genuine improvement rather than sampling noise.  Llama-3.2-3B's final CI $[0.013, 0.027]$ is entirely below 5\%, formally establishing the capacity-floor failure.

\subsection{Two-Proportion Z-Tests}

We tested whether step-0 and step-49 pass rates differ significantly.  Each step provides 128 independent binary outcomes, making the large-sample z-test appropriate.

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Qwen3-8B}: $z = 14.91$, $p < 10^{-49}$ (step 0 vs.\ final); $z = 32.93$, $p \approx 0$ (first-5 mean vs.\ last-5 mean).  Both highly significant.
    \item \textbf{Llama-3.2-3B}: $z = 1.01$, $p = 0.314$ (step 0 vs.\ final); $z = 1.10$, $p = 0.272$ (first-5 vs.\ last-5). \emph{Not significant}: the model did not improve.
    \item \textbf{Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct}: $z = 5.49$, $p = 3.9 \times 10^{-8}$ (step 0 vs.\ final); $z = 12.37$, $p \approx 0$ (first-5 vs.\ last-5). Significant even from an already-high baseline.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Spearman Rank-Correlation (Monotonic Trend)}

Spearman $\rho$ measures the monotonicity of the reward trajectory across 50 steps.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Spearman Rank-Correlation of Reward vs.\ Training Step}
\label{tab:spearman}
\begin{tabular}{lccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & $\rho$ & $p$-value & Significance \\
\midrule
Qwen3-8B              & $+0.923$ & $< 10^{-20}$ & *** \\
Llama-3.2-3B          & $+0.414$ & $1.64 \times 10^{-3}$ & ns (no practical trend) \\
Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct & $+0.954$ & $< 10^{-20}$ & *** \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Qwen3-8B and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct both show near-perfect monotonic improvement ($\rho > 0.92$).  Llama-3.2-3B's $\rho = 0.41$ is technically significant due to sample size but the gradient is negligible (mean reward changes from $\sim$1.3\% to $\sim$2.3\% across 50 steps), confirming stagnation.

\subsection{Chow Structural-Break Test (Phase Transition)}

We used an exhaustive Chow F-test to detect the step index at which the reward trajectory undergoes a structural change in its linear trend.  This operationalises Finding~2 (RL emergence) with a formal test.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Chow Structural-Break Test Results (Phase Transition Detection)}
\label{tab:chow}
\begin{tabular}{lcccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Best Breakpoint} & $F$ & $p$-value & Pre-break $\mu$ & Post-break $\mu$ \\
\midrule
Qwen3-8B              & Step 25 & 32.35 & $< 10^{-10}$ & 0.099 & 0.863 \\
Llama-3.2-3B          & Step 9  &  1.76 & $0.464$ (ns) & 0.016 & 0.018 \\
Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct & Step 9  &  6.36 & $1.2 \times 10^{-5}$ & 0.756 & 0.927 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

For Qwen3-8B the break at step 25 ($F = 32.35$, $p < 10^{-10}$) is statistically incontrovertible: the model shifts from a near-flat regime (mean 9.9\%) to a steep convergence regime (mean 86.3\%), matching the visual phase transition in Figure~\ref{fig:gsm8k_curves}.  Llama-3.2-3B shows no significant break ($p = 0.464$), consistent with the flat reward trace.  For Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct the early break at step 9 corresponds to the model rapidly saturating its already-high starting performance.

\subsection{Effect Sizes (Cohen's \texorpdfstring{$d$}{d}) and Mann-Whitney Tests}

We split each training run at the detected breakpoint and compared the early-phase and late-phase reward distributions.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Effect Sizes and Mann-Whitney U Tests (Early vs.\ Late Phase)}
\label{tab:cohen_mw}
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Model} & \textbf{Cohen's $d$} & \textbf{Magnitude} & $U$ & $p$-value \\
\midrule
Qwen3-8B              & 5.65 & large & 0   & $1.3 \times 10^{-9}$ \\
Llama-3.2-3B          & 0.25 & small & 163 & $0.587$ (ns) \\
Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct & 2.71 & large & 4   & $5.5 \times 10^{-6}$ \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Qwen3-8B's Cohen's $d = 5.65$ (large by conventional thresholds of $d \geq 0.8$) is among the largest effect sizes reportable from 50-step training --- the early and late phases are statistically non-overlapping distributions ($U = 0$, meaning every late-phase reward exceeds every early-phase reward).

\subsection{Cross-Model Comparisons with Bonferroni Correction}

We compared the final 10 steps of each model pair using Mann-Whitney U and Cohen's $d$.  With two comparisons, the Bonferroni-corrected $\alpha = 0.025$.

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Cross-Model Comparison (Final 10 Steps, Bonferroni-Corrected $\alpha = 0.025$)}
\label{tab:cross_model}
\begin{tabular}{llcccl}
\toprule
\textbf{Model A} & \textbf{Model B} & $U$ & $p$-value & Cohen's $d$ & Result \\
\midrule
Qwen3-8B & Llama-3.2-3B          & 0   & $1.6 \times 10^{-4}$ & 14.04 (large) & \textbf{Significant} \\
Qwen3-8B & Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct & 44  & $0.623$ (ns)         & $-$0.53 (medium) & Not significant \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The comparison against Llama-3.2-3B is significant even after Bonferroni correction ($p = 1.6 \times 10^{-4} < 0.025$), with a Cohen's $d$ of 14.04 --- an extreme effect confirming that the 8B model's final performance is in a categorically different regime from the 3B model's persistent failure.  Qwen3-8B and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct are \emph{not} significantly different in the final 10 steps ($p = 0.623$), consistent with both reaching near-perfect accuracy ($\sim$94\% vs.\ $\sim$98\%): both converge to ceiling performance, with the difference between them attributable to Llama's higher starting point rather than different final capability.

\section{Systematic Scaling Study (Ongoing)}
\label{sec:systematic_study}

\subsection{Motivation and Confound Identification}

The headline comparison in Section~\ref{sec:actual_results} mixes three factors simultaneously: Qwen3-8B (\textbf{base}, Qwen family, 8B) vs.\ Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct (\textbf{instruct}, Llama family, 8B) vs.\ Llama-3.2-3B (\textbf{base}, Llama family, 3B).  Model family, instruction-tuning status, and parameter count all vary at once, making it impossible to attribute performance differences to any single cause.  A peer reviewer correctly identified this as a limitation; this section describes the three-block study we are running to resolve it.

\subsection{Factorial Design}

We decompose the comparison space into three independent blocks, each varying exactly one factor while holding the others constant:

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{Systematic Scaling Study: Three-Block Factorial Design}
\label{tab:systematic_design}
\begin{tabular}{lllll}
\toprule
\textbf{Block} & \textbf{Varying} & \textbf{Fixed} & \textbf{Runs} & \textbf{Status} \\
\midrule
A & Instruction-tuning (base vs.\ instruct) & Llama-3.1 family, 8B & 2 & \textit{Ongoing} \\
B & Model family (Qwen3 vs.\ Llama-3.1)    & Base, $\sim$8B        & 2 & \textit{Ongoing} \\
C & Parameter count (0.6B -- 30B)           & Qwen3 base            & 6 & \textit{Ongoing} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\paragraph{Block A --- Instruction-tuning isolation (Llama-3.1 family, 8B).}
Comparing \texttt{meta-llama/Llama-3.1-8B} (base, no RLHF) against \texttt{meta-llama/Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct} (Meta's instruction-tuned variant) holds family and size constant and isolates the effect of pre-training alignment.  The hypothesis is that instruction tuning provides a higher step-0 pass rate (head-start), accelerating convergence but not changing final accuracy.

\paragraph{Block B --- Family isolation (base models, $\sim$8B).}
Comparing \texttt{Qwen/Qwen3-8B} (base) against \texttt{meta-llama/Llama-3.1-8B} (base) holds instruction-tuning status and size constant.  This isolates the effect of pre-training data and architecture (Qwen3 vs.\ Llama-3.1 RLHF-free base weights).

\paragraph{Block C --- Size ladder (Qwen3 base family, GSM8K).}
Running six Qwen3 base models --- 0.6B, 1.7B, 4B, 8B, 14B, 30B-MoE (3B active) --- holds family and instruction-tuning status constant while varying parameter count.  The primary metrics are (a)~step-0 pass rate (does the model cross the $\approx$7\% capacity threshold?) and (b)~steps to reach 80\% accuracy (convergence speed as a function of $\log(\text{params})$).

\subsection{Experimental Configurations}

Three new configurations have been added to the repository for the runs not yet executed:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Block A/B:} \texttt{configs/gsm8k\_llama\_8b\_base.yaml} --- trains \texttt{meta-llama/Llama-3.1-8B} (base) with identical hyperparameters to the existing instruct run (\texttt{lr}=4e-5, LoRA rank 32, 50 steps, $n$=128).
    \item \textbf{Block C (small):} \texttt{configs/gsm8k\_qwen\_0\_6b.yaml} --- trains \texttt{Qwen/Qwen3-0.6B} (LoRA rank 16, \texttt{lr}=6e-5).
    \item \textbf{Block C (near-threshold):} \texttt{configs/gsm8k\_qwen\_1\_7b.yaml} --- trains \texttt{Qwen/Qwen3-1.7B} (LoRA rank 16, \texttt{lr}=5e-5).
\end{itemize}

The four existing runs (Qwen3-4B, Qwen3-8B, Qwen3-14B, Qwen3-30B-MoE) have been re-tagged to \texttt{wandb\_group: "systematic-scaling-study"} so all runs appear in a single WandB group for side-by-side comparison.

\subsection{Analysis Plan}

Once all new runs complete, we will apply:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Block A (IT effect):} Mann-Whitney U and Cohen's $d$ on final-10-step rewards, comparing Llama-3.1-8B base vs.\ instruct.
    \item \textbf{Block B (family effect):} Same tests, comparing Qwen3-8B vs.\ Llama-3.1-8B (both base).
    \item \textbf{Block C (size ladder):} Plot step-to-80\%-accuracy vs.\ $\log_{10}(\text{params})$; fit a linear scaling law; identify the parameter count below which convergence fails.
    \item \textbf{Omnibus 2$\times$2 ANOVA:} Using final-10-step means as the dependent variable, test main effects of family and instruction-tuning, and their interaction, using the \texttt{two\_way\_anova\_2x2()} function in \texttt{tinker\_atropos/stats\_utils.py}.
\end{enumerate}

All analysis code is in \texttt{notebooks/systematic\_scaling\_study.ipynb}.

\section{Key Findings and Analysis}
\label{sec:key_findings}

\textbf{Finding 1 --- Model Capacity Threshold.}
Llama-3.2-3B completely failed to learn GSM8K (reward $<$3\% throughout training), while Qwen3-8B (base) reached 100\%. The critical difference: at initialization, Qwen3-8B already solves $\sim$7\% of problems correctly, providing GRPO with positive-reward examples to learn from. Llama-3.2-3B generates zero correct solutions, leaving the policy gradient with no signal. This reveals a minimum-capacity requirement for RL to bootstrap mathematical reasoning: the base model must already solve some fraction of the target distribution.

\textbf{Finding 2 --- RL Emergence / Phase Transition.}
Qwen3-8B exhibits a sharp phase transition on GSM8K: after 20 steps of slow progress ($\sim$7--18\%), reward jumps from 18\% to 78\% within five steps, then saturates at 100\% by step 30. This pattern is consistent with the ``aha moment'' phenomenon described in DeepSeek-R1~\cite{deepseek2025r1}: GRPO appears to first learn the answer format, then rapidly internalizes the reasoning strategy once format compliance is stable.

\textbf{Finding 3 --- Instruction Tuning as RL Accelerant.}
Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct starts GSM8K at 79\% (vs.\ 7\% for Qwen3-8B base), demonstrating that Meta's RLHF/instruction-tuning has already solved most of GSM8K. Our GRPO training then pushes it to 100\% in 35 steps with minimal KL divergence. This confirms that RL fine-tuning is complementary to instruction tuning: even highly capable models benefit from task-specific reward optimization.

\textbf{Finding 4 --- MoE Cost-Efficiency.}
Qwen3-30B MoE achieves 99.2\% on GSM8K despite activating only 3B parameters at inference time (10\% of total). The volatile training curve (rewards oscillating 25--100\% in mid-training) is attributable to the sparse routing mechanism: different routing decisions across the 16 group samples produce high-variance gradient estimates. Nevertheless, final performance matches the dense 8B model at a fraction of the inference cost.

\textbf{Finding 5 --- LoRA Sufficiency for Task Mastery.}
All experiments modified only $\sim$0.1\% of total parameters via LoRA (rank 32). Full task mastery on GSM8K (100\% accuracy) was achieved with this minimal parameter budget, confirming that mathematical reasoning patterns in large pre-trained models can be activated through targeted low-rank updates rather than full fine-tuning.

\textbf{Finding 6 --- Benchmark Difficulty Cliff.}
The jump from GSM8K to MATH competition problems reveals a structural difficulty cliff: Qwen3-8B reaches 100\% on GSM8K but only $\sim$14\% on MATH at the same training budget. GSM8K problems require arithmetic and basic algebra; MATH requires proof construction, abstract algebra, and combinatorial reasoning. This suggests that curriculum learning (GSM8K $\rightarrow$ AMC8 $\rightarrow$ AMC10 $\rightarrow$ AIME) may be necessary for competition-level performance.

\textbf{Finding 7 --- GRPO Generalises to Code.}
Applying the same GRPO recipe to HumanEval (binary pass/fail reward from unit test execution) improved Qwen3-3.8B from 32\% to 40\% pass@1 in 50 steps at negligible cost (\$0.50, RTX 4090). The reward signal --- subprocess test execution --- requires no labelled data, making this approach directly applicable to any verifiable code task (security patching, refactoring, API migration). The 8 pp gain from a 3.8B model suggests that larger models trained longer would substantially exceed the 40\% baseline.

\begin{tcolorbox}[colback=purple!5!white, colframe=purple!60!black, title=\textbf{Chapter 5 Key Takeaways (Empirical)}]
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{GRPO works from scratch:} Qwen3-8B base model went from 7\% to 100\% on GSM8K via pure RL---no supervised data needed.
    \item \textbf{Model size threshold confirmed:} Llama-3.2-3B failed completely; 8B is the minimum viable scale for bootstrapping math reasoning via GRPO.
    \item \textbf{Instruction tuning $\times$ RL is complementary:} Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct (79\% $\rightarrow$ 100\%) demonstrates that RL can push already-capable models to task mastery.
    \item \textbf{MoE is compute-efficient:} Qwen3-30B MoE matches dense 8B performance with only 3B active parameters at inference.
    \item \textbf{LoRA sufficiency:} 0.1\% parameter modification achieves full GSM8K mastery---full fine-tuning is not required.
    \item \textbf{MATH requires more:} GSM8K saturates; competition math needs curriculum learning or larger models.
    \item \textbf{GRPO generalises beyond math:} The same recipe applied to HumanEval code generation yields +8 pp pass@1 on Qwen3-3.8B in 50 steps --- no labelled data, \$0.50 compute.
\end{enumerate}
\end{tcolorbox}



\chapter{Limitations and Future Directions}
\label{ch:limitations}

\textit{This chapter provides critical reflection on both the field and our survey. Section~\ref{sec:field-limitations} identifies five structural limitations in current agentic AI research (L1--L5). Section~6.2 acknowledges our survey's own constraints. We conclude with concrete future research directions addressing these gaps.}

\vspace{0.3cm}
\section{Limitations of the Field}
\label{sec:field-limitations}
Digging into the literature, we uncovered a handful of deep-seated limitations baked into how agentic AI research is carried out today. These aren't mere implementation quirks---they point to deeper structural problems in how we evaluate, replicate work, and guard against harm:

\textbf{L1: Benchmark-Reality Gap.} Current benchmarks test simplified versions of real problems. They provide clear goals and measurable success criteria---but real deployment involves ambiguous requirements, adversarial inputs, and cascading failures.

\textit{Example:} An agent scoring 49\% on SWE-bench may have memorized common bug patterns rather than learning general debugging principles. The core issue: we have no validated correlation between benchmark scores and real-world utility. We may be overfitting to evaluation suites rather than making genuine progress.

\textbf{L2: Reproducibility Constraints.} State-of-the-art results (DeepSeek-R1's 79.8\% on AIME 2024, Claude-3.5's 49\% on SWE-bench) are achieved with proprietary training data, undisclosed hyperparameters, and compute budgets exceeding academic accessibility. Our experimental framework deliberately targets reproducibility with open models (Llama-3-8B, Qwen2.5-7B) and documented configurations, accepting lower absolute performance for methodological transparency.

\textbf{L3: Safety-Capability Tradeoff.} Every capability improvement expands the potential for harm \cite{yin2024os}. Agents capable of debugging code can introduce vulnerabilities; agents capable of web navigation can exfiltrate data. Today's safety guardrails (Constitutional AI, RLHF-based refusal) offer only partial cover. Formally verifying agent behavior at scale is still an unsolved puzzle.

\textbf{L4: Metric Inadequacy.} Task completion rates---the dominant metric---obscure reasoning quality. An agent may succeed through luck or fail despite correct reasoning for 90\% of the trajectory. Step-wise evaluation methods exist~\cite{lightman2023lets}, though few teams have adopted them in practice.

\textbf{L5: Computational Barriers.} PPO requires 4$\times$ base model memory; even GRPO consumes substantial GPU-hours for convergence. Those bills slow down iteration cycles and lock out researchers who lack big-iron clusters.

\section{Limitations of This Survey}
A few caveats apply to our analysis:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Temporal Scope:} The core survey analyzes papers from 2023--2025. We include foundational references from earlier years (e.g., PPO~\cite{schulman2017ppo}, Constitutional AI~\cite{bai2022constitutional}) for context, but systematic coverage focuses on the target period.
    \item \textbf{Language Bias:} We mostly stuck to English-language publications, so relevant work from non-English research groups may have slipped under the radar.
    \item \textbf{Publication Bias:} Our analysis tilts toward wins; dead ends and negative findings are scarce in the published record, so they're scarce here too.
    \item \textbf{Verification Limitations:} We quote numbers straight from the papers, no independent replication. Some of those figures may not hold up in practice.
    \item \textbf{Rapid Obsolescence:} The field evolves rapidly; some findings may be superseded by the time of publication.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Training Instabilities in RL-Based Agent Training}
\label{sec:training_instabilities}

Compute bills aside, RL training for agentic systems comes with a few landmines that practitioners need to spot and defuse:

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\caption{Common Training Instabilities in Agentic RL (2024--2025)}
\label{tab:training_instabilities}
\small
\begin{tabular}{@{}p{2.5cm}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{5.5cm}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{4.5cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Instability} & \textbf{Description} & \textbf{Mitigation Strategy} \\
\midrule
\textbf{Echo Trap} & Agent learns to repeat successful patterns verbatim, losing generalization. Manifests as high training accuracy but poor transfer to novel inputs. & Temperature annealing, diversity regularization, periodic curriculum reset \\
\addlinespace
\textbf{Entropy Collapse} & Policy entropy drops prematurely, causing the agent to commit to suboptimal actions before exploring alternatives. & Entropy bonus scheduling, exploration curricula, DAPO-style dynamic sampling \\
\addlinespace
\textbf{Reward Instability} & Learned reward models drift during training, causing the policy to chase moving targets. Common in RLHF pipelines. & Reward model freezing, ensemble rewards, periodic reward calibration \\
\addlinespace
\textbf{Gradient Spikes} & Large, unexpected gradient magnitudes destabilize training. Especially problematic in long-horizon tasks. & Gradient clipping, LayerNorm, learning rate warmup, PPO's trust region \\
\addlinespace
\textbf{Tool Hallucination} & Agent generates syntactically invalid or semantically inappropriate tool calls. Worsens under distribution shift. & GRPO-TCR regularization, tool validation layers, structured generation \\
\addlinespace
\textbf{Credit Assignment Failure} & Sparse rewards in long trajectories prevent learning which actions contributed to success. & Hindsight Experience Replay, dense intermediate rewards, temporal difference methods \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\paragraph{Echo Trap Deep Dive} The Echo Trap (also related to model collapse \cite{shumailov2023curse}) is sneaky---it inflates training metrics while gutting real-world usefulness. When an agent discovers that a specific phrasing or action sequence consistently yields rewards, it may ``lock in'' that pattern regardless of context. Detection requires held-out evaluation sets with significant distributional differences from training data.

\paragraph{Tool Hallucination} \textit{Tool hallucination refers to when agents generate syntactically invalid or semantically inappropriate tool calls that don't match available API specifications.} This phenomenon worsens under distribution shift, requiring regularization techniques like GRPO-TCR to mitigate.

\paragraph{Entropy Collapse Prevention} GRPO's group-relative normalization naturally combats entropy collapse by ensuring diverse trajectories within each batch, but explicit entropy bonuses remain necessary for stable long-term training. The recommended approach combines an initial high entropy coefficient (0.1) with gradual decay to 0.01 over training.

\subsection{Observed Emergent Behaviors from RL Training}
\label{sec:emergent}

One of the more eye-opening discoveries from recent RL-trained agents is the pop-up of clever behaviors nobody coded in. This matters because it reveals what RL optimization actually teaches: not just specific actions, but general problem-solving strategies. When test-based rewards drive systematic debugging or compute-penalties drive cost-aware tool selection, the takeaway is that reward signals can mold meta-cognitive strategies. These emergent behaviors provide insights into what's learnable:

\textbf{Code Self-Correction in Testing Loops:} Agents trained on SWE-bench develop iterative debugging strategies: writing initial fixes, running tests, analyzing failures, and refining solutions---without explicit training on this loop. The behavior emerges from outcome supervision alone, a sign that test-based rewards nudge agents toward methodical debugging.

\textbf{Strategic Tool Selection:} Rather than always using the most powerful tool, RL-trained agents learn context-dependent selection. For simple calculations, they use basic operations; for complex queries, they invoke specialized APIs. This cost-aware behavior emerges from compute-penalized rewards without explicit efficiency training.

\textbf{Reasoning Pattern Regularities:} DeepSeek-R1 and similar GRPO-trained models develop consistent reasoning templates: problem decomposition, intermediate verification, and confidence calibration. These patterns pop up across problem types, hinting that RL can uncover broadly useful meta-cognitive tricks.

\textbf{Error Anticipation:} Agents exposed to adversarial tool environments (via AT-GRPO) learn proactive error handling---checking preconditions before actions, validating outputs before proceeding, and maintaining fallback plans. This defensive programming style emerges from training on environments with realistic failure modes.

\textbf{Communication Protocol Development (Multi-Agent):} In multi-agent settings without predefined communication channels, agents develop shorthand protocols for common coordination patterns. These emergent ``languages'' are task-specific but remarkably consistent across training runs, hinting that the push to coordinate keeps landing agents on the same answer.

What does this mean in practice? Carefully crafted rewards can coax out nuanced skills even when we never hard-coded them. The flip side is that unprogrammed behaviors are trickier to foresee and harder to inspect---an alignment headache we cannot ignore.

\subsection{Research Gaps Analysis}

Sifting through the literature, we spot glaring gaps between what today's best systems can do and what production-grade agents actually need:

\caption{Research gap landscape for agentic AI (Jan 2025). Gaps are positioned by research maturity (x-axis) and deployment impact (y-axis). The \textbf{Critical Priority} quadrant requires immediate attention: safety, benchmarks, and robustness.}
\label{fig:research_gaps_quadrant}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Gap 1: Benchmark-Reality Divide.} Current benchmarks (SWE-bench, WebArena) represent sanitized slices of real-world complexity. Agents achieving 35\% on these benchmarks face qualitatively different challenges in production: ambiguous requirements, adversarial users, and cascading failures. Closing this gap requires benchmarks with realistic noise, incomplete specifications, and multi-stakeholder constraints.

\textbf{Gap 2: Compositional Generalization.} Agents trained on task distributions struggle with novel compositions of familiar primitives. An agent that can ``search'' and ``click'' may fail at ``search then click on the third result.'' Such fragility hints that today's RL losses chase surface-level patterns instead of building transferable primitives.

\textbf{Gap 3: Long-Horizon Credit Assignment.} Current methods (PPO, GRPO) falter when assigning credit past the 10--20 step mark. Hierarchical RL and intrinsic motivation offer partial solutions, yet no current solution can dependably handle 100-step tasks demanding sustained, coherent planning.

\textbf{Gap 4: Robustness-Capability Frontier.} Increasing agent capability systematically increases potential for harm. Current approaches treat safety as a post-hoc constraint rather than an architectural principle. We need architectures that are safe by design---ones where boosting capability doesn't widen the attack surface.

\section{Future Research Directions}

\textbf{Latent Reasoning:} Current CoT approaches expose reasoning in output tokens, adding latency and token costs. Research into latent reasoning---where models ``think'' internally without generating visible tokens---might slash latency without sacrificing reasoning accuracy.

\textbf{Emergent Multi-Agent Coordination:} Moving beyond hand-designed role assignments toward agents that self-organize, develop specialized roles, and establish communication protocols through learning remains an open challenge.

\textbf{Continual Learning:} Agents that adapt to changing environments, learn from deployment feedback, and avoid catastrophic forgetting would push practical usefulness up a notch.

\textbf{Verifiable Reasoning:} Connecting RL-trained reasoning with formal verification (producing proofs or certificates of correctness) could unlock trustworthy autonomy for high-stakes deployments.

\textbf{Efficient Exploration:} Developing exploration strategies that balance curiosity-driven learning with safety constraints, especially when agents touch the live world, is a must.

\begin{tcolorbox}[colback=red!5!white, colframe=red!60!black, title=\textbf{Chapter 6 Key Takeaways}]
\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Benchmarks $\neq$ deployment:} No validated correlation between scores and real-world utility.
    \item \textbf{Reproducibility crisis:} SOTA results require undisclosed data + compute beyond academic reach.
    \item \textbf{Safety-capability tension:} Every capability gain expands harm potential---no free lunch.
    \item \textbf{Open problems:} Verifiable reasoning, continual learning, and emergent coordination remain unsolved.
\end{enumerate}
\end{tcolorbox}



\chapter{Conclusion}
\label{ch:conclusion}

\section*{Summary of Findings}
This systematic review of 60+ papers on reinforcement learning for autonomous AI agents documents a rapidly advancing field. The 2024--2025 period saw researchers pivot sharply from prompt-only techniques toward RL-driven fine-tuning, with benchmark performance improving 4--29$\times$ across major evaluation suites (SWE-bench: 1.7\%$\rightarrow$49\%, WebArena: 6.1\%$\rightarrow$44.8\%, OSWorld: 18.4\%$\rightarrow$73.3\%).

\section*{Key Takeaways}

\noindent\textbf{1. The GRPO Paradigm Shift.} GRPO and its spin-offs now dominate, accounting for 39\% of published work. By eliminating the value/critic network (using group normalization) and leveraging verifiable rewards without a learned reward model, GRPO halves memory requirements (policy + reference model only, vs.\ four networks for PPO). This efficiency enabled DeepSeek-R1 to achieve 79.8\% on AIME 2024---putting capable agent training within reach of smaller labs. The proliferation of specialized variants (Flow-GRPO for multi-turn dialogue, M-GRPO for hierarchical coordination, AT-GRPO for adversarial robustness) suggests this lightweight approach will define near-term development.

\noindent\textbf{2. Tool Mastery Is Now Baseline.} Agents without retrieval and tool-calling abilities encounter fundamental capability limitations. The emergence of RL-optimized tool learning frameworks---ReTool achieving 67\% on AIME 2024, DeepAgent executing 100+ step trajectories---demonstrates that tool integration is now essential for production deployment.

\noindent\textbf{3. Multi-Agent Coordination Matures.} When single agents fail, coordinated multi-agent systems often succeed. MATPO's 18.38\% improvement over single-agent baselines and Mobile-Agent-v3's 73.3\% on OSWorld show that RL-trained multi-agent coordination is now production-ready. \textit{Caveat:} These gains come with coordination overhead---increased latency, $\mathcal{O}(n^2)$ communication costs for fully-connected topologies, and failure propagation risks---that deployment teams must factor into system design.

\noindent\textbf{4. Emergent Capabilities and Risks.} RL training produces behaviors not explicitly programmed: code self-correction, strategic tool selection, communication protocol development, and cross-validation strategies. Put differently, a well-shaped reward can unlock nuanced skills---yet these unplanned behaviors are difficult to anticipate or audit, presenting significant challenges for alignment research.

\section*{Implications for Research and Practice}

For \textbf{researchers}, we flag seven gaps that matter because they block real-world deployment---they are not mere academic footnotes. The benchmark-reality divide means improvements on sanitized benchmarks don't translate to messy reality—this undermines the entire evaluation paradigm. Long-horizon planning fails beyond 20 steps because credit assignment breaks down—this is algorithmic, not a data problem. Safety verification remains informal because formal methods face state-space explosion—this is computational complexity, not implementation. Multi-modal grounding fails because vision and language representations don't align naturally—this is architectural. Sample efficiency remains low because every environment interaction is expensive—this is economic. Interpretability fails because emergent behaviors are unpredictable—this is foundational. Each gap reflects a structural constraint, not a missing feature.

For \textbf{practitioners}, we recommend a three-stage training pipeline (SFT$\rightarrow$RL$\rightarrow$RAG) with GRPO as the default algorithm for resource-constrained settings. Our ablation grid enables systematic evaluation of design choices within typical academic compute budgets.

For \textbf{policymakers}, the rapid capability gains documented here---with minimal additional training overhead---make it clear that governance frameworks need to land before mass rollout. The emergent-behavior findings, in particular, deserve a close look.

\section*{Broader Impact}

Autonomous AI agents mark a tectonic shift---from passive tools to goal-chasing systems. Tools respond to commands; agents pursue objectives over extended time horizons without step-by-step instruction. This is not just incremental improvement—it's a different paradigm. The implications are profound because the failure modes are different: tools fail by not responding; agents fail by pursuing the wrong goal, by getting stuck in local optima, by taking unsafe shortcuts. The risks scale with capability because the action space grows exponentially with horizon length. This creates a safety-capability frontier: capability improvements without safety measures are dangerous; safety measures that cripple capability are useless. The tradeoff is structural, not optional. This means:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Positive:} Democratized automation of complex knowledge work, improved accessibility, and augmented human capability across software engineering, research, and creative domains.
    \item \textbf{Risks:} Potential for cascading failures in agentic chains, difficulty auditing emergent behaviors, and amplification of existing biases in autonomous decision-making.
\end{itemize}

The current approach of capability-first, safety-later is fundamentally flawed. Once you have a capable system, retrofitting safety is like adding seatbelts to a car after it's built—possible, but much harder than designing them in from the start. Worse, emergent behaviors are unpredictable. You cannot bolt on interpretability after the fact if the system's reasoning is opaque. Robustness cannot be added as a layer if the core architecture is brittle. Safety verification cannot be done post-hoc if the state space is intractable. These must be co-equal objectives from the beginning because they constrain the design space. A system optimized purely for capability will inevitably use shortcuts that violate safety—this is optimization pressure, not malice. The only solution is to bake in constraints from the start.

\section*{Future Directions}

Our planned experiments will rigorously test the hypotheses presented in this survey: comparing Llama-3-8B and Qwen2.5-7B across PPO, DPO, and GRPO on standardized benchmarks, with ablations isolating the contributions of tool integration, augmented retrieval strategies, and multi-agent coordination.
 We hope other researchers will replicate, extend, and critique what we have started here.

The goal isn't abstract or academic. Building agents that remain both capable and safe over long, messy interactions is the defining challenge because shorter interactions are already solved. The fundamental issue is scaling: as interaction length grows linearly, the probability of failure grows exponentially. Each step introduces new error modes: tool failures, misinterpretations, compounding inaccuracies, unexpected states. A 100-step interaction isn't 10x harder than a 10-step interaction—it's exponentially harder because error recovery must work across much longer horizons. "Messy" matters because clean, well-defined tasks are already tractable. The real world is ambiguous, adversarial, and constantly changing. Solving this requires agents that can handle uncertainty, detect their own failures, and recover autonomously. This isn't incremental progress; it's crossing a threshold from narrow AI to systems that can actually operate in the real world.

\chapter{Glossary of Acronyms}
\label{app:glossary}

\begin{longtable}{@{}p{2.5cm}p{5cm}p{7cm}@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Acronym} & \textbf{Full Term} & \textbf{Brief Definition} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Acronym} & \textbf{Full Term} & \textbf{Brief Definition} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\textit{Continued on next page...}} \\
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Core Algorithms}} \\
\midrule
PPO & Proximal Policy Optimization & RL algorithm using clipped objective to ensure stable policy updates \\
DPO & Direct Preference Optimization & Optimizes models using preference pairs directly without needing a separate reward network
 \\
GRPO & Group Relative Policy Optimization & Memory-efficient RL using within-batch ranking instead of critic network \\
RLHF & RL from Human Feedback & Alignment framework using human preferences as a guiding reward signal
 \\
TRPO & Trust Region Policy Optimization & PPO's predecessor with hard KL constraint \\
SFT & Supervised Fine-Tuning & Initial training stage using demonstration data \\
\midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Architectures \& Techniques}} \\
\midrule
RAG & Retrieval-Augmented Generation & Combining LLM generation with external knowledge retrieval \\
CoT & Chain-of-Thought & Prompting technique eliciting step-by-step reasoning \\
ReAct & Reasoning + Acting & Framework interleaving reasoning traces with environment actions \\
MDP & Markov Decision Process & Mathematical framework for sequential decision-making \\
POMDP & Partially Observable MDP & MDP where agent cannot observe full state \\
PRM & Step-Level Reward Model & evaluates individual deduction steps rather than just the final result \\
ORM & Outcome Reward Model & Scores only final answers/outcomes \\
\midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Benchmarks}} \\
\midrule
SWE-bench & Software Engineering Bench & Evaluates code repair on real GitHub issues \\
WebArena & Web Arena & Tests web navigation and interaction tasks \\
OSWorld & Operating System World & Desktop GUI automation benchmark \\
GAIA & General AI Assistants & Multi-tool reasoning benchmark (466 tasks) \\
AIME & American Invitational Math Exam & Competition math problems used to evaluate reasoning \\
\midrule

\multicolumn{3}{l}{\textbf{Metrics \& Concepts}} \\
\midrule
KL & Kullback-Leibler (divergence) & Measures difference between probability distributions \\
TSR & Task Success Rate & Primary metric: fraction of tasks completed correctly \\
$\epsilon$ & Epsilon (clipping threshold) & PPO hyperparameter bounding policy change (typically 0.1--0.2) \\
$\beta_{\text{KL}}$ & Beta (KL penalty) & PPO/GRPO coefficient constraining drift from reference model \\
$\beta_{\text{DPO}}$ & Beta (preference strength) & DPO hyperparameter controlling preference signal strength \\
$\gamma$ & Gamma (discount factor) & Weights future vs. immediate rewards (0--1) \\
\end{longtable}



\chapter{Hyperparameter Reference}
\label{app:hyperparams}

This appendix provides recommended hyperparameter ranges based on best practices from the surveyed literature. Values represent starting points; task-specific tuning is expected.

\section{PPO Hyperparameters}

\begin{table}[htbp]
\centering
\caption{PPO hyperparameter recommendations for agentic fine-tuning}
\label{tab:ppo_hyperparams}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lccl@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Parameter} & \textbf{Symbol} & \textbf{Recommended Range} & \textbf{Notes} \\
\midrule
Clipping threshold & $\epsilon$ & 0.1--0.2 & Lower for stability, higher for exploration \\
Discount factor & $\gamma$ & 0.99--0.999 & Higher for long-horizon tasks \\
GAE lambda & $\lambda$ & 0.95--0.99 & Bias-variance trade-off in advantage estimation \\
Value loss coefficient & $c_1$ & 0.5--1.0 & Relative weight of critic vs. policy loss \\
Entropy coefficient & $c_2$ & 0.001--0.01 & Encourages exploration; decay over training \\
Learning rate & $\alpha$ & 1e-6 to 5e-5 & Smaller for larger models \\
Batch size & --- & 32--512 & Memory-constrained; larger improves stability \\
Mini-batch size & --- & 4--64 & Gradient accumulation if memory-limited \\
PPO epochs & --- & 2--10 & Per batch; more risks overfitting \\
KL penalty coefficient & $\beta_{\text{KL}}$ & 0.01--0.1 & Optional; constrains drift from reference \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\section*{Inference Compute Estimates}

\begin{table}[htbp]

\caption{Inference requirements for agentic evaluation}
\label{tab:compute_inference}
\begin{tabular}{@{}lcccc@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Benchmark} & \textbf{Tasks} & \textbf{Avg. Steps/Task} & \textbf{GPU-Hours (7B)} & \textbf{GPU-Hours (70B)} \\
\midrule
SWE-bench Verified & 500 & 15--30 & 8--16 & 40--80 \\
WebArena & 812 & 10--25 & 10--20 & 50--100 \\
OSWorld & 369 & 20--40 & 12--24 & 60--120 \\
AIME 2024 & 30 & 1--5 & 0.5--1 & 2--5 \\
GAIA & 466 & 5--15 & 4--8 & 20--40 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\section*{Academic Resource Recommendations}

For researchers with limited compute budgets:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Minimum viable setup:} 2$\times$ RTX 4090 (24 GB each) with QLoRA can fine-tune 7-billion parameter models using GRPO
    \item \textbf{Recommended academic setup:} 4$\times$ A100 (40 GB) enables full fine-tuning of 7-billion parameter models and QLoRA for 13B
    \item \textbf{Cloud alternatives:} Lambda Labs, RunPod, and Vast.ai offer A100s at \$1--2/GPU-hour for burst workloads
    \item \textbf{Free resources:} Google Colab Pro+ provides 40 GB A100 access; Kaggle offers 30 GPU-hours/week
\end{itemize}



\chapter{Reproducibility Checklist}
\label{app:reproducibility}

This checklist supports reproducibility of \textit{our proposed experiments} described in Chapter~\ref{ch:experiments}, which use open-weight models (Llama-3-8B, Qwen2.5-7B) with documented configurations. We follow NeurIPS reproducibility guidelines and ML community best practices.

\textit{Note: This checklist applies to our reproducible experimental framework---not to proprietary systems surveyed in the literature (GPT-4, Claude-3.5, etc.) for which open weights, training data, and full hyperparameters are unavailable.}

\section*{Code and Data Availability}

\begin{tabular}{@{}p{0.7\textwidth}cc@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Item} & \textbf{Status} & \textbf{Location} \\
\midrule
Training code with configuration files & \ding{51} & GitHub repository \\
Evaluation scripts for all benchmarks & \ding{51} & GitHub repository \\
Trained model checkpoints & \ding{51} & HuggingFace Hub \\
Preprocessed datasets & \ding{51} & HuggingFace Datasets \\
Raw experimental logs (WandB) & \ding{51} & WandB project link \\
Random seeds for all experiments & \ding{51} & Config files \\
Hardware specifications & \ding{51} & This appendix \\
Software dependencies (requirements.txt) & \ding{51} & GitHub repository \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}

\vspace{0.3cm}
\noindent\textbf{Repository:} \url{https://github.com/pes-llm-research/agentic-rl-finetuning}

\noindent\textit{Note: Repository will be made public upon project completion. Access available to examiners upon request.}

\section*{Experimental Protocol Checklist}

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=*]
    \item \textbf{Randomization}
    \begin{itemize}[nosep]
        \item[$\square$] Fixed random seeds: 42, 123, 456 (three runs per experiment)
        \item[$\square$] Seed controls: PyTorch, NumPy, Python random, CUDA
        \item[$\square$] Deterministic algorithms enabled where possible
    \end{itemize}

    \item \textbf{Hyperparameter Selection}
    \begin{itemize}[nosep]
        \item[$\square$] Grid search over learning rate: \{1e-6, 5e-6, 1e-5\}
        \item[$\square$] Grid search over batch size: \{16, 32, 64\}
        \item[$\square$] Validation set for early stopping (10\% held out)
        \item[$\square$] Final hyperparameters documented in Appendix~\ref{app:hyperparams}
    \end{itemize}

    \item \textbf{Statistical Reporting}
    \begin{itemize}[nosep]
        \item[$\square$] Mean $\pm$ standard deviation across 3 seeds
        \item[$\square$] 95\% confidence intervals via bootstrap (1000 resamples)
        \item[$\square$] Statistical significance tests (McNemar's, $p < 0.01$)
        \item[$\square$] Effect sizes reported (Cohen's $d$ for continuous metrics)
    \end{itemize}

    \item \textbf{Benchmark Evaluation}
    \begin{itemize}[nosep]
        \item[$\square$] Official evaluation harnesses used (no custom metrics)
        \item[$\square$] Test sets never seen during training or validation
        \item[$\square$] Identical prompts across all compared methods
        \item[$\square$] Temperature = 0 for deterministic generation during eval
    \end{itemize}

    \item \textbf{Compute Transparency}
    \begin{itemize}[nosep]
        \item[$\square$] Total GPU-hours reported per experiment
        \item[$\square$] Carbon footprint estimated (ML CO2 Impact calculator)
        \item[$\square$] Wall-clock time logged
        \item[$\square$] Hardware failure/restart documentation
    \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

\section*{Known Limitations and Failure Modes}

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Benchmark variance:} SWE-bench results can vary $\pm$3\% across runs due to LLM non-determinism even with temperature=0 (floating-point ordering effects)
    \item \textbf{Environment drift:} WebArena uses live websites; results may not reproduce exactly if site layouts change
    \item \textbf{API dependencies:} Some experiments require API access (OpenAI, Anthropic) which may have version-dependent behavior
    \item \textbf{Hardware sensitivity:} Results on consumer GPUs (RTX 4090) may differ from A100 due to different numerical precision handling
\end{itemize}



\chapter{Paper-to-Taxonomy Mapping}
\label{app:paper_mapping}

This appendix provides a comprehensive mapping of all surveyed papers to our 19-category taxonomy (Figure~\ref{fig:rl_taxonomy}). The mapping enables systematic analysis of research distribution across the field and supports reproducibility of our literature classification.

\section*{Taxonomy Categories Reference}
\begin{multicols}{2}
\footnotesize
\noindent\textbf{Core Algorithms}
\begin{enumerate}
\item Policy-Based Methods
\item Value-Based Methods
\item Model-Based Methods
\end{enumerate}

\noindent\textbf{Learning Paradigms}
\begin{enumerate}
\setcounter{enumi}{3}
\item RLHF \& Alignment
\item Imitation Learning
\item Offline RL
\end{enumerate}

\noindent\textbf{Advanced Methods}
\begin{enumerate}
\setcounter{enumi}{6}
\item Multi-Agent RL
\item Hierarchical RL
\item Meta-Learning
\item Safe RL
\item Exploration
\end{enumerate}

\columnbreak
\noindent\textbf{Applications}
\begin{enumerate}
\setcounter{enumi}{11}
\item Agentic RL
\item Robotics
\item Reasoning
\item Security \& Safety
\item Benchmarks
\end{enumerate}

\noindent\textbf{Infrastructure}
\begin{enumerate}
\setcounter{enumi}{16}
\item Frameworks
\item Surveys
\item Open-Ended Learning
\end{enumerate}
\end{multicols}

\chapter*{Notation}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Notation}
\label{ch:notation}

\noindent Table~\ref{tab:notation} summarizes the mathematical notation used throughout this survey. Standard reinforcement learning conventions follow \cite{sutton2018reinforcement}.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{@{}cl@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Symbol} & \textbf{Description} \\
\midrule
$\mathcal{S}, \mathcal{A}, \Omega$ & State, action, and observation spaces \\
$\pi_\theta(a|s)$ & Policy parameterized by $\theta$; $s$ may denote belief-state/history embedding \\
$\pi_{\text{ref}}$ & Reference policy (frozen) \\
$r(s,a)$ & Reward function (lowercase $r$ preferred; some papers use $R$) \\
$H$ & Episode horizon (number of timesteps) \\
$\gamma$ & Discount factor $\in [0,1]$ \\
$\beta_{\text{KL}}$ & KL penalty coefficient (PPO/GRPO) \\
$\beta_{\text{DPO}}$ & DPO preference strength/temperature parameter \\
$\epsilon$ & PPO clipping threshold (typically 0.1--0.2) \\
$\hat{A}_t$ & Advantage estimate at time $t$ \\
$G$ & Group size for GRPO \\
$y_w, y_l$ & Preferred and non-preferred outputs (DPO) \\
$T(s'|s,a)$ & Transition dynamics \\
$h_t$ & Observation history up to time $t$ \\
$\alpha$ & Learning rate (also denoted $\eta$ in some literature) \\
$\text{KL}[\cdot\|\cdot]$ & Kullback-Leibler divergence \\
$\sigma(z)$ & Logistic sigmoid: $\sigma(z) = 1/(1+e^{-z})$ \\
$s_G$ & Group standard deviation (within-batch) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\caption{Mathematical notation used in this survey. See individual chapters for extended definitions. Note: $\beta$ is overloaded in RL literature; we distinguish $\beta_{\text{KL}}$ (KL penalty) from $\beta_{\text{DPO}}$ (preference strength) where context requires clarity.}
\label{tab:notation}
\end{table}

\section{Tinker Checkpoint Download}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{wave3_seed.pdf}
\caption{Wave 3 multi-seed DeepSeek-V3.1 reproducibility (from W\&B).}
\label{fig:wave3_seed}
\end{figure}
\label{sec:tinker-checkpoints}

Tinker checkpoints use the format \texttt{tinker://<run-uuid>:train:0/sampler\_weights/final}.
To download a checkpoint:

\begin{lstlisting}[language=Python]
import tinker
client = tinker.Client()
future = client.download_checkpoint(
    "tinker://<run-uuid>:train:0/sampler_weights/final"
)
with open("model-checkpoint.tar.gz", "wb") as f:
    f.write(future.result())
\end{lstlisting}

\section{HuggingFace Model Cards}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{top20.pdf}
\caption{Top 20 experiments ranked by last-10 average reward.}
\label{fig:top20}
\end{figure}
\label{sec:hf-models}

Published model cards follow the template at \texttt{huggingface/MODEL\_CARD\_TEMPLATE.md}.

\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \textbf{PPO Qwen3-8B}: \texttt{arvindcr4/tinker-rl-bench-ppo\_gsm8k\_Qwen3-8B\_s42}
\item \textbf{Madhu GRPO HumanEval}: \url{https://github.com/madhukumara1993/qwen3-grpo}
\end{itemize}

\section{W\&B Run Summary}

\begin{figure}[htbp]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{heatmap.pdf}
\caption{Reward heatmap across all 76 W\&B-tracked experiments (step $\times$ run).}
\label{fig:heatmap}
\end{figure}
\label{sec:wandb-summary}

All training curves, reward traces, and loss plots are logged to:

\begin{center}
\url{https://wandb.ai/arvindcr4-pes-university/tinker-rl-lab-world-class}
\end{center}

Key run groups:
\begin{itemize}[nosep]
\item \texttt{campaign\_v2\_w1\_*} --- Wave 1: cross-architecture frontier scaling (8 models, 0.5B--397B)
\item \texttt{campaign\_v2\_w2\_*} --- Wave 2: group size ablation ($G \in \{2, 4, 16, 32\}$)
\item \texttt{campaign\_v2\_w3\_*} --- Wave 3: multi-seed DeepSeek-V3.1 reproducibility
\item \texttt{wave6\_w6\_*} --- Wave 6: Qwen3-8B sensitivity (temperature, rank, batch size)
\item \texttt{w1\_llama33-70b-inst\_*} --- Llama-3.3-70B-Instruct 4-seed replication
\item \texttt{ppo-gsm8k-*} --- PPO head-to-head comparisons (Modal H100)
\end{itemize}
